### Adv 1 -- Climate Change 

#### Climate change causes extinction through [cascading]{.underline}, [complex]{.underline} system failure. 

Dr. Catherine **Richards et. al 24**. Consultant, McKinsey; PhD,
Engineering and Energy Transition, Cambridge. Dr. Richard Lupton: Senior
Lecturer, Sustainability and Climate Change, Department of Medical
Engineering, University of Bath; PhD, University of Cambridge. Dr.
Julian Allwood: Professor of Engineering and the Environment, University
of Cambridge; Lead Author, 5th Assessment Report of the
Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change; PhD, Imperial College London.
Dr. SJ Beard: Senior Research Associate, University of Cambridge;
Borysiewicz Interdisciplinary Fellow; PhD, Philosophy, London School of
Economics. Dr. Luke Kemp: Research Affiliate & Former Research
Associate, University of Cambridge; hono(u)rary (lol) lecturer,
environmental policy, Australian National University; PhD, International
Relations, Australian National University. "Re-Framing the Threat of
Global Warming: An Empirical Causal Loop Diagram of Climate Change, Food
Insecurity and Societal Collapse" and "Existential Change: Lesson from
Climate Change for Existential Risk." Chapters 13 and 14 in *An
Anthology of Global Risk*. Open Book Publishers. 2024.
https://doi.org/10.11647/OBP.0360.

**[Within]{.underline}** [Existential Risk Studies it is common to
hear]{.underline} people ask [the question "[is ﻿ climate change]{.mark}
an ﻿ [existential]{.mark} risk[?]{.mark}"]{.underline}, and
[many]{.underline} who ask this question [answer
**negatively**]{.underline}, [arguing]{.underline} that [as a result ﻿
climate change is not an important topic of research **within the
field**]{.underline}. [However]{.underline}, whether it is [answered
affirmatively or not,]{.underline} [this question [is
**misguided**]{.mark}]{.underline}. There are three reasons for thinking
this. [Firstly]{.underline}, [it makes little sense on a
**probabilistic** level]{.underline}; whether something will be [a
threat to]{.underline} our [collective existence is not]{.underline} a
[**binary** matter]{.underline}, [it is a question of
likelihood]{.underline}. However, **[many researchers]{.underline}**
[within Existential Risk Studies **mistakenly**]{.underline} [conflict ﻿
existential risk with **events**]{.underline} [that could be existential
﻿catastrophes]{.underline}. [Secondly]{.underline}, ﻿ [[climate change is
not a **single uniform process**]{.underline}]{.mark} that will affect
everyone in the same way; [[it is]{.mark} a set of **[diffuse
impacts]{.mark}**]{.underline} [to different]{.underline} ﻿ exposed
[populations]{.underline}, [interacting with different ﻿ vulnerabilities
and ﻿ exposures]{.underline}, and [[activating different **risk ﻿
cascades**]{.underline}]{.mark}. As Richards et al. show, [[it]{.mark}
will inevitably [interact with]{.mark} a **host** of [other
threats]{.mark} (not only ﻿ **[food security]{.mark}** and **[societal
collapse]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [but]{.underline} even [factors such
as]{.underline} the explosivity of ﻿ [[volcanic eruptions]{.mark}
or]{.underline} the [emergence of **[zoonotic
pathogens]{.mark}**),]{.underline}1 and [[these]{.mark} can
**interact**]{.underline} with one another [to [create
**reinforcing**]{.mark}]{.underline} [feedback loops or
**"[global]{.mark} systems [death spirals]{.mark}"**.]{.underline}2
Finally, "﻿ existential risk" is too vague and arbitrary a concept for
the question to ever be answered. All the definitions of ﻿ existential
risk that have received the greatest public attention thus far, such as
Toby ﻿ Ord's, focused not in terms of an impact on humanity at any point
in time but rather in terms of "the loss of long-term future value";3
either referring to the author(s) particular vision of a high-tech
intergalactic utopia, or a fuzzy undefined idea of "our potential".4

Other [[authors]{.mark} have practised]{.underline} attribution
**[substitution]{.underline}** [and sought to [answer]{.mark} an
**easier question**]{.underline} [such as ["will]{.mark} the **[direct
impacts]{.mark}** of ﻿ climate change [make]{.mark} the [**Earth
uninhabitable**?"]{.mark}]{.underline} [as a proxy for existential
risk]{.underline},5 [or suggested agricultural
**impossibility**]{.underline} [as a **proxy** for ﻿ civilisational
collapse at a given level of temperature rise]{.underline}.6 [[These
are]{.underline}]{.mark} certainly [more tractable
questions]{.underline}, but [they are also [**entirely different**
questions]{.mark}]{.underline}, and [there is a **danger**]{.underline}
[in thinking that answering them is **sufficient**]{.underline} [to
assess]{.underline} the [overall level of ﻿ **climate
risk.**]{.underline}

We are better off reverting back to the common-sense definition of ﻿
existential risk as the risk to the existence of a given object, and
specifying whether the object under threat is humanity as a whole (﻿
extinction risk), global industrial society (collapse risk), or
something else entirely. We should be thinking of an overall level of
risk emergent from a particular socio-ecological system, and how much ﻿
climate change influences this level.7 And the question we should be
asking about this risk is what contribution, under certain scenarios, ﻿
climate change will make, bearing in mind that it will almost certainly
be operating in tandem with many other drivers of risk.

Considering [this]{.underline} revised question can also [help to
**rectify**]{.underline} [a **recurring** problem in the ﻿ climate risk
literature: using mean global temperature rise as the **sole** threat
indicator]{.underline}. Authors and ﻿ activists alike have frequently
made a direct link between the level of warming and the likelihood of
global ﻿ catastrophe, with 4--6 °C being most frequently used as this
terrible threshold.8 However, [global surface
**temperature**]{.underline} [is **only one**]{.underline} [of the ﻿
climate]{.underline} change induced [factors we need to worry about. 3
°C of warming]{.underline} above pre-industrial levels [could
be]{.underline} entirely **[manageable]{.underline}** [if]{.underline}
it occurs [in a world of adaptive technologies]{.underline}, high levels
of [multilateral cooperation]{.underline}, [wealth
equality]{.underline}, [trust in institutions]{.underline},
[and]{.underline} the [safe management of other ﻿ planetary
boundaries]{.underline}. [It could]{.underline} also [be
**catastrophic**]{.underline} [in a world where other ﻿ planetary
boundaries are **transgressed**]{.underline}, [the international order
is **riven with conflict**]{.underline}, lethal autonomous weapons are
in mass production, [and societies are scarred by
**inequality**]{.underline}, low trust, [and
**polarisation**]{.underline}. Understanding the contribution of ﻿
climate change to Global Catastrophic Risk requires a more sophisticated
approach which looks beyond the direct impacts of a given level of
warming to think through fully formed climate scenarios. We believe
that, when conceived of in this way, the risks associated with ﻿ climate
change are more appreciable and it is far harder to argue that
understanding them is unimportant; however, even if others disagree with
this assessment, we still maintain that this is the right way to think
about the problem.

2\. Catastrophic Neglect

Given how poorly questions about [[catastrophic]{.mark} ﻿ climate
change]{.underline} are often framed, it is hardly surprising that it
[has been a highly neglected subject of study]{.underline}, not only
among ﻿ existential risk researchers but also among ﻿ climate change
researchers. Even at the basic level of temperature rise scenarios, [we
give **far more attention**]{.underline} [to]{.underline} studying the
impacts of [lower- end [warming]{.mark} rather than high-end
warming]{.underline}. Text-mining of IPCC reports shows that mentions of
[3 °C and above [is **underrepresented**]{.mark}]{.underline} [relative
to]{.underline} its **[likelihood]{.underline}** ([and
impact]{.underline}),9 a finding that has been verified by both
literature sampling and the reports of popular authors trying to
summarise the climate risk science.10 If anything, [this trend appears
to have **worsened**]{.underline} [over time]{.underline} with
subsequent IPCC reports.111 The [use of **complex**]{.underline} [risk
assessments to study climate scenarios has]{.underline} also [been
**neglected**]{.underline}: [looking at compound hazards is]{.underline}
already **[rare]{.underline}**,12 [let alone considering risk ﻿
**cascades**]{.underline} and integrated climate ﻿ catastrophe
assessments. Yet catastrophic ﻿ climate change remains high on the public
and political agenda, creating both a perception that this is a risk
receiving far more attention than it is, and also an intellectual vacuum
that is easily filled by poor quality research, ranging from speculative
doom-mongering13 to overly simplistic neoclassical economic models.14

[There are four]{.underline} key [reasons for this
oversight]{.underline} of extreme global ﻿ climate risk. First is
international climate policy. The 2015 Paris Climate Agreement on
Climate Change has channelled scientific attention toward the
agreement's goal of limiting warming to 2 °C above pre- industrial
levels and pursuing efforts to stabilise it below 1.5 °C, as these are
now the publicly stated goals of climate ﻿ negotiations (even if they are
highly unlikely to actually be realised). Second, [[analysis]{.mark} of
high-end warming]{.underline} scenarios [and complex risk assessments
[are]{.mark} simply **[harder]{.mark} to do**]{.underline}. [The higher
the warming gets]{.underline}, [the more difficult it becomes to
study]{.underline}, as these scenarios are more displaced for the
current climatic niche. Moreover, **[complex]{.underline}** ﻿ climate
[risk assessments involving multiple factors are]{.underline} far [more
**challenging**]{.underline} than a ﻿ hazard-centric analysis focusing on
only the direct impacts of mean global temperature rise. Third,
[[climate scholarship]{.mark} has had a **strong
incentive**]{.underline} [to "[err on the side of **least
drama**]{.mark}".]{.underline}15 [Climate change has
**long**]{.underline} [been the target of [fossil-fuel]{.mark} industry
[campaigns]{.mark} to **[sow doubt]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [not just
on]{.underline} attempts to assess ﻿ climate change's [catastrophic
potential but even the **fundamental science**]{.underline}, and [this
[create]{.mark}s [incentives for **conservative
science**]{.mark}]{.underline} [[that builds
**consensus**]{.underline}]{.mark} [and does not **risk** exploring
divergent hypotheses]{.underline}.16 Finally, [many
**fear**]{.underline} that [discussing extreme risk could]{.underline}
cause people to dwell too much on worst case scenarios,
[breed]{.underline}ing [fatalism]{.underline} and paralysis. However,
[this concern is misplaced]{.underline}; meta-analyses over hopeful vs.
fearful messaging are mixed,17 and in any case this is a false
dichotomy. One of the most referenced pieces for those concerned about
the paralytic effect of fear does show that hopeful messaging is more
poignant than fear but also that "worry" is even more effective than
hope.18 The difference between worry and fear is one of degrees; the
latter could even dissipate into the former over time. Furthermore,
research should not be a PR exercise aimed to sway the public, in open
democracies we have a duty to do honest risk assessments combined with
clear recommendations for what can be done.19

Of course, these factors are only compounded by the consensus procedures
of the IPCC, which seeks to synthesise scientific evidence for political
purposes but is still often held up as a neutral arbiter of climate
science. While useful, these procedures tend to produce lowest
common-denominator outcome, which is precisely what is not needed when
exploring extreme risks.20 This is an important point of reflection for
any future efforts to build similar bodies aimed at bringing scientific
research to bear on the governance of other global risks.

3\. The Risks and Rewards of Responding

Climate change is inherently tractable and we already have the
technologies we need to stop creating it, albeit without the
institutions to fairly distribute them with a sufficient level of
urgency. However, responding to risks like ﻿ climate change can incur
risks of its own. Indeed, the IPCC, in its risk concept notes to the
sixth assessment report, does not just discuss the usual three
determinants of risk, ﻿ hazard, ﻿ vulnerability, and exposure, but also
identifies "﻿ response risks".21 Others have suggested that response
should be added to the classic list of determinants.22 In some cases,
responses may be far worse than the initial perceived risk, that is,
they are iatrogenic: the treatment is worse than the disease.

Existential risk is especially prone to ﻿ response risks due to its
scale, severity, and often speculative ﻿ nature. For instance, at the
extreme a speculative fear of dispersed ﻿ weapons of mass destruction
could justify a mass surveillance state.23 In general, there is always
the potential for concerns over global risk to justify a Stomp Reflex
--- the abuse of emergency powers which inappropriately empower those
atop a hierarchy and shield them from scrutiny. 24 This is also true for
climate change

**[Reacting]{.underline}** [to ﻿ climate change could lead to
**[emergency responses]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [[such as]{.mark} ﻿
stratospheric aerosol injection (﻿ [SAI]{.mark}),]{.underline} in an
attempt [to **manipulate**]{.underline} [the **quantity** of solar
radiation hitting the earth and]{.underline} thus [counter]{.underline}
some of the impacts of ﻿ [climate change]{.underline}. [Existing data
on]{.underline} the direct impacts of ﻿ [SAI and]{.underline} its
[[contribut]{.mark}ion [to ﻿ **systemic risk**]{.mark} or triggering
other ﻿ hazards is **sparse**]{.underline}. Preliminary analysis suggests
that [[the]{.mark} **greatest [problem]{.mark}**]{.underline}
[[is]{.underline}]{.mark} the ﻿ [latent risks of **"﻿ [termination
shock]{.mark}".**]{.underline} [[If]{.mark} a
[calamity]{.mark}]{.underline} such as a nuclear ﻿ war
[**[deactivates]{.underline}** [the system]{.underline}]{.mark} for a
prolonged time, then [[this]{.mark} could [**significantly accelerate**
warming]{.mark}]{.underline}. Hence ﻿ [SAI shifts]{.underline} the ﻿ [risk
distribution by]{.underline} likely lowering the level of risk in an
average scenario but **[fattening the tail]{.underline}** [or
"worst-case" scenarios]{.underline} depending on how ﻿ SAI is deployed,
to what degree it is used, and what geopolitical and ecological world it
is dispersed into.25 On the other hand, there are also frequently
neglected ﻿ co-benefits of climate mitigation policies, such as the
public health benefits of eliminating coal smoke and other pollutants
from our air.26

[Such problems of ﻿ response risk are]{.underline} perhaps [the most
**neglected**]{.underline}. Yet [they are **precisely**]{.underline}
[what the study of ﻿ existential risk **needs to grapple
with.**]{.underline} This could include by using robust decision-making
procedures, such as the minimax principle, to aid in selecting policy
options under ﻿ uncertainty or using ﻿ deliberative democratic processes
to combine ﻿ diverse perspectives and co-create effective policy
responses.

#### [Domino effects]{.underline} create [catastrophic]{.underline} indirect impacts.

Mihai **Andrei 22**. Holds a master's degree in Applied
Geo-Biology/Environmental Protection from the University of Bucharest,
co-founder and CEO of ZME Media, "Could climate change actually wipe out
humanity? Researchers say we need to study this ASAP",
https://www.zmescience.com/ecology/climate/could-climate-change-actually-wipe-out-humanity-researchers-say-we-need-to-study-this-asap/

**[Cascading risks]{.underline}**

Researchers have been explaining that [[climate change
is]{.underline}]{.mark} **[catastrophic]{.underline}** for decades.
Recently, major political figures, including heads of state are
finally starting to acknowledge the magnitude of this threat. Just last
week, President Biden warned that the clock is ticking toward a climate
catastrophe --- a signal that maybe, just maybe, we may need to start
taking climate change seriously. But acknowledging that it's a big
problem is just the first step.

[How big of a problem is it?]{.underline} [Is it [an **existential
threat**]{.mark}? Could it]{.underline} literally **[wipe out
humanity?]{.underline}** Could it change our way of living
fundamentally, bring us back to the Dark Ages, or is it "just" as bad as
the COVID-19 pandemic?

**[At first glance]{.underline}**, [the idea that climate change could
be]{.underline} an [existential]{.underline} threat to humanity [seems
exaggerated]{.underline}. [After all, even in the direst scenarios,
climate change would "only" kill or relocate a few hundred million
people]{.underline}. Setting aside for a moment the sheer magnitude of
that catastrophe, it doesn't sound like something like that could wipe
out billions of people, [right?]{.underline}

[Well]{.underline}, **[society doesn't really work that
way]{.underline}**. Think of the COVID-19 pandemic, for instance. Let's
simplify things and say the virus would have killed 2% of humans on
Earth if left unchecked. We kept it in check somewhat with lockdowns and
restrictions, we've developed a vaccine in a year, and still, it has
caused a huge disruption in our society (a disruption that's still
unfolding). If it had actually killed 2% of the population, the results
would have been catastrophic and would have cascaded in ways that are
hard to predict. Herein lies the problem: [[problems aren't problems in
a **void**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[they create a **domino
effect**]{.underline}]{.mark} [[that]{.underline}]{.mark}
**[[ripples]{.mark} throughout society.]{.underline}**

[If climate change makes some areas **unlivable**]{.underline} (which
it's already doing), [if it affects global food production and global
health]{.underline}, if it causes water scarcity for millions of people,
[this could [translate into]{.mark} **social
[unrest]{.mark}**]{.underline}, **[[war]{.underline}]{.mark}**,
[[and]{.mark} who knows how many [other problems]{.mark}]{.underline}.
[We need to understand **how bad things can get**]{.underline}, the
authors of the new study urge.

"[There are **plenty of reasons** to believe [climate change could
be]{.mark}come **[catastrophic]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [[even at
**modest**]{.mark} **levels of [warming]{.mark}**]{.underline}," [said
lead author Dr. Luke Kemp from Cambridge's Centre for the Study of
Existential Risk]{.underline}. "[[Climate]{.mark} change [has played a
role in **every mass extinction**]{.mark} **event**]{.underline}. [It
has helped **fell empires** and shaped history.]{.underline} Even the
modern world seems adapted to a particular climate niche," he said.

"[[Paths]{.mark} to disaster [are **not limited** to]{.mark} the
**[direct impacts]{.mark}** of high temperatures, such as extreme
weather events.]{.underline} **[Knock-on effects]{.underline}** [such as
**[financial crises]{.mark}**]{.underline},
**[[conflict]{.underline}]{.mark}**, [[and]{.underline}]{.mark} **[new
[disease]{.mark} outbreaks]{.underline}** [[could trigger **other
calamities**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[and]{.underline}]{.mark} impede
recovery from [potential disasters such as **[nuclear
war]{.mark}**]{.underline}."

#### [Geothermal energy]{.underline} is missing link -- it's the best renewable source. 

**Pisciotta 23** \[Max is a PhD candidate concentrating in chemical
engineering, and the winner of our inaugural Graduate Essay Prize.
Pisciotta\'s research focuses on carbon capture and carbon removal
technologies and industrial decarbonization. Prior to starting their PhD
at Penn, they graduated with a BS and MS in mechanical engineering from
the Colorado School of Mines, and briefly worked as a technology analyst
at Accenture. When Pisciotta is not in the lab, they can be found doing
LGBTQ+ advocacy on Penn\'s campus, running, or working to communicate
scientific findings to the public. They are also part of the 2022-2023
cohort of Graduate Associates at Perry World House Geothermal Energy: A
Missing Piece in the Equitable Energy Transition
<https://perryworldhouse.global.upenn.edu/perryworldhouse/news/geothermal-energy-missing-piece-equitable-energy-transition>
Accessed 2/10/2025\]

According to the sixth assessment IPCC report, [[there is unequivocal
proof that human influence has warmed the atmosphere, ocean, and land,
contributing to climate change]{.underline}]{.mark}. This [human
influence is from the emission of greenhouse gases into the atmosphere,
most prominently,]{.underline} carbon dioxide ([CO2]{.underline}). These
[[emissions are generated from all sectors of the economy]{.mark}, from
agriculture and aviation to cement production and steelmaking, via the
use of fossil fuels]{.underline}, namely coal, oil, and natural gas.
[[To have a 66% chance of achieving average global warming less than 1.5
ºC by 2100]{.mark}, an important goal laid out in the Paris Agreement,
[emissions need to be drastically]{.mark} reduced and legacy emissions
in the atmosphere must be removed]{.underline} (IPCC 2021). While some
culprit industries are much easier to decarbonize than others, [due to
recent advancements in technology, a common slogan among the climate
conscious is "electrify everything."]{.underline} The idea is that [one
path to achieving global climate goals is to use electricity to power
all]{.underline} applications and industries because carbon-free
electricity can be generated via renewable energy quite "easily." The
[intermittency of these resources is often addressed using some version
of "there is already existing technology that can be used to store this
electricity easily]{.underline}," i.e., batteries. However, few of these
conversations are discussing just how large battery infrastructure needs
to be to meet seasonal demand and how the raw materials for solar
panels, wind turbines, and batteries are sourced, let alone disposed of
at end-of-life. **[[Within these same conversations, the only renewable
energy resource that has the capacity to generate electricity 24/7, or
baseload power, is rarely mentioned: geothermal
energy]{.mark}]{.underline}** (U.S. DOE 2019). [Geothermal energy is the
energy that comes from the heat deep in the Earth's surface. There are
traditionally three types of geothermal power plants, which are designed
to maximize the energy production from a given resource]{.underline}
based on the formation, fluid temperature, and salinity of the
geothermal fluid (Shulman 1995). Hot geothermal fluid is accessed by
drilling wells (3-7 km deep) into the earth (U.S. DOE 2019), attaching a
pump to draw the hot geothermal fluid up to the surface. It is then used
to power via a thermo-power cycle, often a turbine, which generates
electricity (Shulman 1995). [Geothermal energy is responsible for
producing less than 1% of US electricity today, but with conventional
methods, has the potential to produce more than 8% by 2050]{.underline}
(Geothermal Technologies Office 2021). Other countries that rely on
geothermal energy include Iceland (799 MW), Kenya (\~790 MW), and
Indonesia (2.28 GW). [[Enhanced geothermal systems (EGS) are being
developed to expand access to geothermal energy in regions where deeper
wells are needed to access this resource, to bolster t]{.mark}he
[potential]{.mark}]{.underline} for low-carbon electricity generation.
[Although geothermal energy has many attractive qualities, namely, the
ability to produce baseload, low-carbon electricity, and its wide
expanse of access, it is still deeply rooted in the essence of
extraction. This positionality makes for a contentious
place]{.underline} in the portfolio of solutions to avoid the
detrimental impacts of climate change. Provided that the fossil fuel
industry is underpinned by extraction, [deep parallels between the two
industries can be drawn. However, there are still critical
distinctions]{.underline} that set geothermal energy apart and make it a
valuable addition to a comprehensive strategy for mitigating climate
change. These [deep parallels have the potential to serve a much wider
purpose that should not be ignored; they allow for expertise, skills,
and assets to be transferred from the fossil fuel industry to the
emergent and more climate conscious geothermal energy
industry]{.underline} (Smith 2023). [There is explicit expertise that is
transferrable between the two industries. Geothermal energy is based on
the extraction of geothermal brine and reinjection]{.underline} into an
underground reservoir where the brine is reheated. [Reservoir and
drilling engineers in the oil and gas industries have expertise within
the areas of reservoir dynamics and management, and deep well drilling
and management]{.underline}, respectively. As the demand for
carbon-intensive oil and gas declines and companies begin to constrict,
the [[geothermal energy industry may become home to some of the previous
oil and gas labor force due to the reduced need for
retraining]{.underline}]{.mark}. In addition to expertise and labor
force, the often-proprietary [geologic data collected by oil and gas
companies stands to benefit the geothermal energy industry]{.underline}
as well. Prior to drilling an operational well for oil or gas
extraction, [companies must have the subsurface characterized either via
contract services or in-house. Throughout this process, samples are
taken, analyzed, and geomechanical models are developed; leading to a
lot of data acquisition]{.underline} (Santos, Dahi Taleghani, and
Elsworth 2022). The results from these tests and models are then
analyzed to determine if the subsurface has the potential for
productivity (i.e., oil and gas extraction) and the economics that would
surround this project. Whether or not the project is pursued, the
company has acquired data via the drilling exploration process. The data
collected from this process is not uniquely useful for the oil and gas
industry. Before geothermal energy wells can be drilled, drilling
exploration must also be done to determine the existence of a geothermal
reservoir, formation temperature, and geothermal brine conditions and
composition (Santos, Dahi Taleghani, and Elsworth 2022). Furthermore,
[[there are projects that have begun to evaluate the potential to
repurpose abandoned or retired oil and gas wells]{.mark} for the use in
geothermal energy production. In 2021, the first abandoned oil well was
converted into a closed-loop geothermal system]{.underline} in Hungary
and went on to produce 0.5 MW of thermal energy (Santos, Dahi Taleghani,
and Elsworth 2022). Further demonstration projects are underway in the
U.S. and Slovenia (Santos, Dahi Taleghani, and Elsworth 2022). To
illustrate the vast opportunity for geothermal energy production using
existing abandoned or retired oil and gas wells, in the U.S. alone,
there are 3.7 million abandoned oil and gas wells as of 2020 and only
41% of those have been decommissioned or plugged, according to the U.S.
EPA (Smith 2023). [With the U.S. positioned to repurpose these wells and
institute low-carbon baseload power generation, we have the potential to
lessen the reliance on other renewable energy sources, like solar and
wind, protecting grid stability and bolstering energy
independence]{.underline}.

#### Geothermal energy in the Arctic is [reliable]{.underline} and [linked]{.underline} to [energy security]{.underline} and [diplomacy]{.underline} in the Arctic. 

**Kalantzis '24** \[[Theofanis Kalantzis]{.underline}, Post-Graduate at
the school of Economics, Business and International Studies and European
Studies Department, University of Piraeus. "From Polar Ice to Policy:
The Role of Us and Other Key Actors in Arctic Climate Change, Energy
Transition and Geopolitical Dynamics," Piraeus, 2024.
https://dione.lib.unipi.gr/xmlui/bitstream/handle/unipi/17454/KALANTZIS.pdf?sequence=1#page=49.45\]

[**Geothermal** energy is]{.underline} [an]{.underline}other [important
type of **renewable energy source**]{.underline}, [which has attracted
the interest of many countries]{.underline}, especially in areas with
intense geothermal activity. Iceland, for example, has developed
advanced geothermal power generation and heating systems on the
periphery even though it is not exactly in the Arctic, demonstrating
that geothermal energy can be a central pillar of the energy transition.
[Some Arctic regions]{.underline}, [such as **Russia** and
**Canada**]{.underline}, [have already identified very important
**geothermal fields** in the **Arctic** and have already invested
heavily in this **tech**nology]{.underline}, [and are expected to
**invest more** in this technology]{.underline}. [The interest of
international actors demonstrates in any case that the **exploitation**
of **geothermal energy** in the Arctic can provide continued and
**reliable energy production**]{.underline}, [despite the adverse
climatic conditions]{.underline} prevailing in the region (Kolker et
al., 2022).

In conclusion [the exploitation of **geothermal** energy in the
**Arctic** has an extremely important **geopolitical
dimension**]{.underline}, [as Russia and other countries in the region
seek to expand their **influence** through]{.underline} partnerships and
[investments]{.underline} in renewable energy projects. [At the same
time]{.underline}, [the development of **geothermal projects** in the
region is **linked** to the **increase** in **energy security** and the
**strengthening** of the **diplomacy** of the Arctic
countries]{.underline}.

#### New government support supercharges geothermal innovation -- That's key to breaking fossil-fuel dependency. 

**Kaufman 4-4** \[[Alexander C. Kaufman]{.underline}, award-winning
reporter who has covered energy, climate change, and geopolitics for
more than a decade. "The Hottest Thing in Clean Energy," The Atlantic,
April 4, 2025.
https://www.theatlantic.com/science/archive/2025/04/geothermal-energy-boom/682291/\]

[In the **U**nited **S**tates]{.underline}, **[geothermal
energy]{.underline}**, which uses the Earth's heat to create
electricity, [supplies less than **half of 1 percent** of the country's
electricity]{.underline}, [but few other clean-energy sources offer as
much promise]{.underline} right now. Many climate activists support
geothermal energy as a renewable power source that generates zero-carbon
electricity. [A recent report]{.underline} from the Rhodium Group, an
energy-research firm, [projected that geothermal could meet as much as
**64 percent** of **new** electricity **demand**]{.underline} from data
centers [by the early 2030s]{.underline}. [America is far behind rivals
such as **China** and **Russia** in manufacturing solar panels or
building nuclear plants]{.underline}. [But **geothermal** makes use of
an area of the **U.S. industrial base** that has **grown** in recent
years]{.underline}---[oil and gas production]{.underline}.

Cindy Taff, whose company, Sage Geosystems, is anticipating geothermal's
potential growth, told me about a recent drive she took through southern
Texas that illustrated that overlap. "The same drilling rig that drilled
our well in September was on a lease right off the highway drilling an
oil-and-gas well," she said, laughing. "It's just the same."

Taff came from the oil industry: She was once a vice president at Royal
Dutch Shell who commanded a team of 350 employees using hydraulic
fracturing (better known as fracking) to drill their way through five
countries' bedrock. Fracking had driven an oil-and-gas boom starting in
the mid-2000s, and her team had looked at using the same technique to
tap the Earth's underground heat. At Shell, "we never actually drilled
wells" to try it on geothermal energy, she told me. "It was
frustrating." The opportunity looked big enough to her that she started
Sage.

Much like oil and gas, [**geothermal** energy]{.underline}, which
harnesses the planet's molten core to make steam, [had long been
**confined** to]{.underline} the places where access came easy---[the
American **West**]{.underline}, where Yellowstone's famous geysers hint
at the heat below, or volcanic Iceland. In those places---generally
volcanic hot spots where magma flows at shallow depths in the Earth's
crust and underground water reservoirs---geothermal energy can be a
substantial source of power. Currently, it provides roughly 10 percent
of Nevada's electricity generation and as much as 5 percent of the power
California produces; Iceland generates 30 percent of its electricity,
and Kenya nearly half, from geothermal. Traditional coal or nuclear
plants generate heat to turn water into steam, which spins turbines to
make electricity. Geothermal power stations do the same using hot water
from underground reservoirs.

[Sage uses **fracking** **tech**nology to crack open hot rocks even
deeper underground]{.underline}, [enabling access to heat in **more
locations**]{.underline}. The company's drillers then inject water into
the well, prying open the stone fissures and creating an artificial
reservoir. When Sage releases that water, the pressure from underground
shoots it upward, and the heat creates vapors that spin turbines and
crank out electricity. This system can also serve as storage for
weather-dependent wind and solar: Extra electricity from turbines and
panels can pump water into Sage's wells that can be released later to
produce electricity.

Sage expects to have its first energy-storage facility up and running in
Texas in the coming weeks, but already has a deal to sell power to
Meta's data centers. And a similar start-up, Fervo Energy, demonstrated
that it could use fracking technology to successfully produce 24/7
carbon-free energy back in 2023, at a pilot project in Nevada.

[Geothermal does have certain **advantages** compared with other sources
of renewable energy]{.underline}. [Solar and wind need **large areas**
of land]{.underline}, [huge volumes of **minerals**]{.underline}, [and a
massive new network of **transmission lines**]{.underline}. (Plus, China
dominates those industries' supply chains.) [Hydroelectric dams are less
dependable in a world where water is growing scarcer]{.underline} and
precipitation harder to forecast. [Nuclear reactors cost billions of
dollars and take years]{.underline} to build; the U.S. depends heavily
on countries such as Canada, Kazakhstan, and Russia for uranium fuel,
and has yet to establish the infrastructure to either permanently store
or recycle nuclear waste.

For now, most of the efforts to debut next-generation geothermal
technology are still in the American West, where drilling is relatively
cheap and easy because the rocks they're targeting are closer to the
surface. But if the industry can prove to investors that its power
plants work as described---which experts expect to happen by the end of
the decade---geothermal could expand quickly, just like oil-and-gas
fracking did.

[That the]{.underline} "enhanced" [**geothermal** industry piggybacks on
**tech**nology from the fossil-fuel industry also puts it in a position
to **grow**]{.underline}. "In the U.S., our manufacturing base is
falling apart. But we have a ridiculously good industrial base in oil
and gas," Charles Gertler, who until recently worked at the Energy
Department's Loan Programs Office and co-authored a report outlining a
pathway for the industry's growth, told me. "The fact that you can just
rely on many of the same tools and people and technologies and supply
chains is the reason a lot of folks are so excited." [Investors have
been cool on the industry since a handful of conventional geothermal
projects went under two decades ago]{.underline}. [But Gertler estimated
that]{.underline}, [if **five** to **10 new geothermal projects** prove
**successful**]{.underline}, [banks will **open** their **wallets**
again]{.underline}.

[Unlike other renewable-energy sources]{.underline}, [the emerging
geothermal sector has received **little direct support** from the
**federal government**]{.underline}. By the time Fervo had demonstrated
it could frack for geothermal energy, the Biden administration had
already passed two monumental climate-spending laws, which directed
billions of dollars toward technologies such as solar, wind, and nuclear
power, but just \$84 million for early-stage geothermal. Companies such
Fervo and Sage could still benefit, though, from tax credits for
producing zero-carbon electricity, if Republicans in Congress don't
repeal key parts of Biden's Inflation Reduction Act.

"I don't know where that's going," Representative Celeste Maloy, the
Utah Republican in whose district Fervo is building its first
large-scale plant, told me. But she said [slashing the requirements for
obtaining **federal permits**]{.underline}---which her party is eager to
do---[could give the industry enough of a boost]{.underline} that "it
almost doesn't matter what happens to the IRA incentives." (No company
has made that case to her, she allowed.)

Like any energy industry, geothermal has external costs that could
become bigger issues as it grows. In 2017, an early experiment in
enhanced geothermal energy in South Korea triggered a serious
earthquake. (Earthquakes doubled in Texas in 2021 thanks to oil and gas
companies injecting sludgy wastewater into underground wells.) Locations
with particularly good hot-rock resources could end up overlapping with
threatened species, just as one of the nation's biggest lithium projects
ran up against an endangered wildflower or one of California's largest
solar farms put tortoises at risk. Environmentalists primed to see
anything with Big Oil's fingerprints on it as suspicious will find
plenty of connections between this industry and fossil-fuel companies.
And, as the industry scales up, it will use larger volumes of water.

Fervo, for one, has been pushing to use water too brackish for
agricultural or municipal purposes, Tim Latimer, Fervo's chief
executive, told me: "So we're not really fighting with people over water
even though we're in the western desert." Other companies, such as XGS
Energy, are boring more conventional wells and keeping water contained
in closed-loop pipes, eliminating the risk of losing any water at all in
the process.

[Electricity has to come from somewhere]{.underline}, though, and as
demand surges, the Trump administration is winning over support even
from some Democrats to keep coal plants open longer. Meanwhile, [gas
power plants are **expanding**]{.underline}. To keep the lights
on---while keeping utility bills and global temperatures down as much as
possible---[the country will need to employ all available resources of
**clean power**]{.underline}, an[d perhaps especially those the current
administration is willing to support]{.underline}.

climate targets.**

#### [Finally, every degree]{.underline} of mitigation matters. Trump puts stable pathways in [jeopardy]{.underline} but doesn't [guarantee]{.underline} irreversible climate change.

#### \-\--Extra Impact

#### Independently, Geothermal innovation spurs the green hydrogen revolution. 

**Collins '24** (Gabriel, Baker Botts fellow in energy and environmental
regulatory affairs at Rice University's Baker Institute for Public
Policy. "Alaska Geothermal Power Can Fuel U.S. Indo-Pacific Strategy,
Opportunities on the Pacific Rim can kick-start a critical sector,"
October 7, 2024,
https://foreignpolicy.com/2024/10/07/alaska-geothermal-military-china/)

[[Geothermal energy offers the 24/7/365 **baseload electricity**]{.mark}
supplies that countries need for building out and operating competitive
industrial bases]{.underline}. Because it can continually run regardless
of weather or sunlight, every megawatt of geothermal power that comes
online can displace coal, gas, or oil-based dispatchable generation.
[Climate benefits follow.]{.underline} Furthermore, unlike hydropower
and many other thermal power plant types, [[**geothermal** is
substantially decoupled from drought risk]{.underline}]{.mark}. It is
also potentially capable of load-following to fill gaps in wind and
solar generation, a capability that Sage Geosystems has recently
demonstrated at megawatt-scale.

[[Geothermal generation's engagement]{.mark} of physical heat [also
opens possibilities for supporting food cultivation]{.mark} in
greenhouses [and distillation of seawater]{.mark}.]{.underline} [Where
warranted by remoteness (Aleutian Islands) or by strategic circumstances
(Taiwan), [geothermal power can also potentially support **green
hydrogen production and liquid fuel synthesis**]{.mark}.]{.underline}

[Geothermal power also brings security benefits. Policymakers are
recognizing in the wake of Russia's invasion of Ukraine that during
industrial warfare, energy assets can and will be targeted.]{.underline}
Fossil fuel generation facilities on islands are the most vulnerable
because an adversary can trigger blackouts through interdicting seaborne
fuel imports and does not even need to strike on land to create
potentially strategic effects. This is true for vital U.S. territories,
including the Aleutian islands of Unalaska and Adak; Shemya Island;
Guam; and Hawaii.

**[Only]{.underline} [scalable hydrogen]{.underline} meets [smart
city]{.underline} energy demands---no other energy source is feasible.**

**Hassan et al. '23** \[Qusay Hassan; affiliated with the Department of
Mechanical Engineering at the University of Diyala. Aws Zuhair Sameen;
faculty at the College of Medical Techniques at Al-Farahidi University.
Olushola Olapade; PhD Candidate in Energy Engineering at AGH University
of Krakow. Mohammad Alghoul; Senior Research Scientist at King Fahd
University of Petroleum and Minerals. Hayder M. Salman; Head of
Artificial Intelligence Sciences at Al-Turath University. Marek
Jaszczur; Professor of Energy and Fuels at AGH University of Krakow.
\"Hydrogen fuel as an important element of the energy storage needs for
future smart cities.\" *International Journal of Hydrogen Energy* 48,
no. 78 (2023): 30247-30262. hpd\]

Is hydrogen being the best energy storage for smart cities?

The first inhabitants are anticipated to move into the net-zero-emission
megacity of the future, where smart cities have already begun to expand.
Even if wind and solar photovoltaic are the anticipated contributions,
**[[it is very unlikely]{.mark} that [smart cities would be]{.mark}
entirely [powered by renewable]{.mark} energy sources
\[105\].]{.underline}** Renewable energy sources such as **[[wind and
solar]{.underline}]{.mark}** photovoltaic **[energy [cannot be
sent]{.mark} where they are needed, [and there is no]{.mark} commercial
[way to store]{.mark} large amounts of energy for a long
time]{.underline}** to meet grid needs. Although the problem could have
been solved with dispatchable renewable energy, such as concentrated
solar power, which stores thermal power internally and allows
dispatchability \[\[106\], \[107\], \[108\]\], a grid that uses only
uses solar and wind energy needs external energy storage, which is not
available off-the-shelf.

Since inconsistent and unexpected power output from wind turbines and
photovoltaic arrays cannot meet grid demand alone in the absence of
energy storage, **[energy storage is unquestionably the biggest
challenge facing renewable energy today]{.underline}**. Unfortunately,
this problem receives no attention and nothing is being done to create
the tools required to address it. **[[Only a small]{.mark} [number
of]{.mark} the very few energy storage [tech]{.mark}nologies that have
been offered so far [are]{.mark} technologically [ready, and
none]{.mark} of them [are good enough to meet]{.mark} the [needs of
smart cities]{.mark} right now.]{.underline}**

The acceptable option like pumped hydro energy storage has a slower
reaction time and poorer round-trip energy storage efficiency than
batteries, ranging around 80% \[\[109\], \[110\], \[111\]\].
Additionally, it is difficult to execute without a river or lake. As
there has not been much research done on saltwater-pumped hydro energy
storage, and there are still numerous obstacles to be addressed in the
particular area, this would be a somewhat innovative idea. Other energy
storage options are possible in theory, but they are more like pleasant
concepts than functional off-the-shelf items due to their relatively low
degree of technological preparedness. Flywheels, stacked blocks,
batteries, and outdoor thermal storage are notable examples of these
non-traditional energy storage methods. Excess wind and solar
Photovoltaic energy are used to warm the molten salt, which is then
utilized in a power cycle using Rankine or supercritical CO2, for
example, in concentrated solar \[112\], despite the fact that it is not
yet a commercially accessible product and has a low round-trip
efficiency of less than 50%.

**[Energy storage in [hydrogen is]{.mark} the [best]{.mark} replacement
for battery energy storage or a superior addition to battery energy
storage]{.underline}**. In recent years, **[[battery]{.mark} energy
[storage has unfairly]{.mark} competed]{.underline}** with hydrogen
energy storage, **[which has [attracted]{.mark} far [more
financial]{.mark} and political [backing, pushing hydrogen]{.mark}
energy storage [into the background]{.mark}]{.underline}**.
Nevertheless, despite the vast prospects for additional advancement,
**[[the technical]{.mark} [readiness]{.mark}]{.underline}** level of
individual technologies for hydrogen generation, storage, delivery,
**[[and usage is]{.mark} unquestionably [high]{.mark}]{.underline}**. We
reiterate that the energy **[storage industries for [batteries and
hydrogen are]{.mark} not competing in any way, but rather
[complementary]{.mark} and synergistic]{.underline}** technologies from
a net-zero viewpoint \[113,114\]. Therefore, the two most promising
technologies: battery energy storage and hydrogen energy storage should
be the foundation of energy storage of smart cities. Both of these
technologies are currently under development. Electrolyzers are
well-known goods; electrolyzers, which are readily available items, have
attained efficiencies of about 80% \[115\]. Aside from being stored and
later converted back into energy using more conventional combustion
systems or fuel cells, the hydrogen that smart cities ultimately create
may also be sold for applications such as transportation fuel or others.
While electrolyzers produce electricity using hydrogen as fuel, hydrogen
fuel cells produce electricity using hydrogen as the fuel. **[[Fuel cell
tech]{.mark}nology [is]{.mark} likewise [well-established]{.mark}
\[116\]. The [efficiency]{.mark} of fuel cells has also increased,
[reaching 80%]{.mark}]{.underline}** for somewhat higher round-trip
efficiencies. **[Both fuel cells and electrolyzers have the [potential
to further increase]{.mark} their efficiency.]{.underline}**

**[Hydrogen may be used to generate energy in gas turbines, fuel cells,
and boilers]{.underline}**. The expelled gas is a water vapor after
burning in an oxygen-containing combination, with no harmful emissions.
**[Hydrogen gas is necessary to synthesize using CO2-\[carbon dioxide\]
capture techniques from energy sources or different
substances]{.underline}**, such as water, **[by converting carbon fuel
into a clean and carbonless fuel]{.underline}**. Both methods cost a lot
of money and waste a lot of energy, which is annoying.

Planning more hydrogen production for exportation may help the grid
balance by taking into account cross-, interannual, and multidecadal
variation, as well as maintenance needs or power plant breakdown. The
delivery of wind and solar photovoltaic energy to energy storage may
enable the generation of excess hydrogen that is not used to generate
electricity that is delivered back to the grid when needed, but rather
exported. Since the demand for hydrogen is clearly expanding, however,
this requires more solar PV, wind, and electrolyzer capability, which is
lucrative \[117,118\]. It is possible to see hydrogen energy storage as
more than just a way to stabilize the smart city grid of renewable
energy. Wind and solar power plants could have higher outputs, and
electrolyzers could be used to make hydrogen, which could then be sent
abroad.

Renewable energy is only likely to help in balancing. For the best
results, which include complete grid demand at the lowest levelized
price of electricity without carbon dioxide emissions, energy storage
can also contribute from external thermal energy storage, batteries,
and, in some cases, seawater pumped hydro energy storage. Hydrogen
energy storage should, however, predominate. Whereas the energy
conversion efficiency of hydrogen energy storage using electrolyzers and
fuel cells is undoubtedly lower than that of lithium-ion batteries,
**[we make the case that [hydrogen]{.mark} energy storage, which has
other advantages, [can be the answer to the]{.mark} [immediate]{.mark}
energy [storage problem for smart cities]{.mark}]{.underline}**. The
sooner the energy storage problem is accepted, the more likely it is
that the renewable energy-only grid of the smart city will move toward a
permanent solution rather than adopting temporary workarounds such as
the use of electricity generated from the combustion of hydrocarbon
fuels purchased along with carbon credits. **[Without energy storage, [a
system that uses exclusively renewable energy is not feasible]{.mark},
and without hydrogen, energy storage is also not
feasible]{.underline}**. If we want to use only renewable energy in the
future, we should pay more attention to how important hydrogen and
battery storage are \[119,120\]. It is important to note that hydrogen
can also be utilized to make hydrocarbon fuels such as methane, which
employ CO2 that is collected during their combustion as feedstock in
power plants that use more conventional combustion fuels but only have
carbon capture \[121,122\]. It has been acknowledged that hydrogen,
together with biofuels and electric vehicles, is one of the three main
low-carbon transportation options. Hydrogen is a valuable fuel that can
readily replace fossil fuels, petroleum, and natural gas. By using
hydrogen, the negative effects of biofuels on the environment, land use,
and the short range and long charging times of electric cars can be
lessened \[123\].

Germany planning to invest \$10.6 billion, to France, and Portugal
putting forward \$8 billion each, even countries on the African
Continent like Namibia, investing \$9.4 billion on green hydrogen
production commenting operations in 2026 with over 300,000 tons of
production. Also, the British government anticipates spending around
\$16.6 billion and Japan \$3 billion, and China invested \$16 billion to
make industries greener \[124\].

Hydrogen energy with integrated system for future smart cities

**[An integrated system for [hydrogen]{.mark} energy [in smart cities
could include]{.mark}]{.underline}** the following components.

- Hydrogen **[[production]{.underline}]{.mark}**: Renewable sources such
  as solar or wind power can be used to produce hydrogen through
  electrolysis. This process splits water into hydrogen and oxygen,
  producing a clean and sustainable source of fuel.

- Hydrogen [**[storage]{.underline}**:]{.mark} Hydrogen can be stored
  **[in tanks or pipelines for later use]{.underline}**. This allows for
  flexibility in the use of hydrogen energy, as it can be produced and
  stored during times of excess renewable energy production and used
  during times of peak energy demand.

- **[[Fuel cells]{.underline}]{.mark}**: Fuel cells use hydrogen and
  oxygen to produce electricity, with only water and heat as byproducts.
  Fuel cells can be used to power a range of applications, from
  buildings to transportation.

- **[Smart [grid integration]{.mark}]{.underline}**: A smart grid can be
  used **[to manage the flow of hydrogen energy and optimize its
  use]{.underline}**. This includes monitoring energy demand, managing
  energy storage and distribution, and ensuring energy security.

- **[[End-use applications]{.underline}]{.mark}**: Hydrogen energy can
  be used in a range of applications, **[[including heating]{.mark} and
  [cooling]{.mark} systems, [transport]{.mark}ation, [and power
  generation for]{.mark} buildings and other
  [infrastructure]{.mark}.]{.underline}**

An integrated system for hydrogen energy in smart cities can help to
reduce carbon emissions and improve energy security. However,
**[[significant]{.mark} infrastructure [investments will be required to
develop and deploy]{.mark} this system [at scale.]{.mark}]{.underline}**

**Sustainable cities prevent [extinction]{.underline}.**

**Cribb '17** \[Julian; Senior Visiting Fellow @ FutureDirections,
Former Scientific Editor @ The Australian, Author of *The Coming Famine*
and *Poisoned Planet*, Former Foundation President @ Australian Science

**[By the mid-twenty-first century]{.underline}** the world's
**[[cities]{.underline}]{.mark}** will be home to approaching eight
billion inhabitants and will carpet an area of the planet's surface the
size of China. **[Several]{.underline}** megacities will have 20, 30,
and even 40 million people. The largest city on Earth will be
Guangzhou-Shenzen, which already has an estimated 120 million citizens
crowded into in its greater metropolitan area (Vidal 2010).

By the 2050s **[these]{.underline}** colossal conurbations **[will
absorb 4.5 trillion tonnes of fresh water]{.underline}** for domestic,
urban and industrial purposes, **[and [consume]{.mark}]{.underline}**
around **[[75 billion tonnes of]{.underline}]{.mark}** metals, materials
and **[[resources]{.mark} every year]{.underline}**.
**[Their]{.underline}** very **[[existence will depend
on]{.underline}]{.mark}** the **[[preservation of]{.mark} a precarious
[balance between]{.mark} the essential [resources]{.mark} they need for
survival and growth]{.underline}**---**[[and]{.mark}]{.underline}** the
**[[capacity of the Earth to supply them]{.underline}]{.mark}**.
Furthermore, **[they will generate]{.underline}** equally **[phenomenal
volumes of waste, reaching an alpine 2.2 billion tonnes by
2025]{.underline}** (World Bank)---an average of six million tonnes a
day---and probably doubling again by the 2050s, in line with economic
demand for material goods and food. In the words of the Global Footprint
Network "**[The [global]{.mark} effort for [sustainability will be won,
or lost, in]{.mark} the world's [cities]{.mark}]{.underline}**" (Global
Footprint Network 2015).

As we have seen in the case of food (Chap. 7), **[these giant [cities
exist on a razor's edge, at risk of resource crises]{.mark} for which
none of them are fully-prepared]{.underline}**. **[[They are]{.mark}
potential [targets for w]{.mark}eapons of [m]{.mark}ass
[d]{.mark}estruction]{.underline}** (Chap. 4). They are **[[humicribs
for]{.mark} emerging pandemic [diseases]{.mark},]{.underline}** breeding
grounds for crime **[and [hatcheries for unregulated]{.mark} advances in
[biotech]{.mark}nology,]{.underline}** nanoscience, chemistry **[and
[a]{.mark}rtificial [i]{.mark}ntelligence.]{.underline}**

**[Beyond]{.underline}** all **[this]{.underline}**, however,
**[[they]{.mark} are]{.underline}** also **[the places where human minds
are joining]{.underline}** at lightspeed **[to share
knowledge]{.underline}**, wisdom **[and [craft solutions to]{.mark} the
multiple [challenges]{.mark} we face.]{.underline}**

**[For good or ill, in cities is the future of civilisation
written]{.underline}**. They cradle both our hopes and fears.

Urban Perils

The Brazilian metropolis of **[Sao Paulo is a harbinger
of]{.underline}** the **[challenges]{.underline}** which lie ahead for
Homo urbanus, Urban Human. In a land which the New York Times once
dubbed "the Saudi Arabia of water" because its rivers and lakes held an
eighth of all the fresh water on the planet, Brazil's largest and
wealthiest city and its **[20 million inhabitants were almost brought to
their knees by a]{.underline}** one-in-a-hundred-year
**[drought]{.underline}** (Romero 2015). It wasn't simply a drought,
however, but rather a complex interplay of factors **[driven by human
overexploitation of the]{.underline}** surrounding
**[landscape]{.underline}**, **[pollution of the]{.underline}**
planetary **[atmosphere]{.underline}** and biosphere, corruption of
officialdom, mismanagement **[and governance failure]{.underline}**. In
other words, **[the sort of mess that potentially confronts most of the
world's megacities.]{.underline}**

In the case of Sao Paulo, climate change was implicated by scientists in
making a bad drought worse. This was compounded by overclearing in the
Amazon basin, which is thought to have reduced local hydrological
cycling so that less water was respired by forests and less rain then
fell locally. This reduced infiltration into the landscape and inflow to
river systems which land-clearing had engorged with sediment and
nutrients. Rivers running through the city were rendered undrinkable
from the industrial pollutants and waste dumped in them. The Sao Paulo
water network leaked badly, was subject to corruption, mismanagement and
pilfering bordering on pillage. Government plans to build more dams
arrived 20 years too late. "Only a deluge can save São Paulo," Vicente
Andreu, the chief of Brazil's National Water Agency (ANA) told The
Economist magazine (The Economist 2014). Depopulation, voluntary or
forced, loomed as a stark option, officials admitted. Although the
drought eased in 2016, water scarcity remained a shadow over the
region's future.

**[Sao Paulo is far from alone: many of the world's great cities face
the spectre of thirst]{.underline}**. The same El Nino event also struck
the great cities of California, leading urban planners---like others all
over the world---to turn to desalination of seawater, using electricity
and reverse osmosis filtration (Talbot 2014). This kneejerk response to
unanticipated water scarcity echoed the Australian experience where,
following the 'Millennium Drought' desalination plants were producing
460 gigalitres of water a year in four major cities (National Water
Commission 2008)---only to be mothballed a few years later when the dry
eased. By the early 2010s there were more than 17,000 desalination
plants in 150 countries worldwide, churning out more than 80 gigalitres
(21 billion US gallons) of water per day, according to the International
Desalination Association (Brown 2015). Most of these plants were powered
by fossil fuels which supply the immense amount of energy needed to push
saline water through a membrane filter and remove the salt. Ironically,
by releasing more carbon into the atmosphere, desalination exacerbates
global warming and so helps to increase the probability of fiercer and
more frequent droughts. It thus defeats its own purpose by reducing
natural water supplies. A similar irony applies to the city of Los
Angeles which attempted to protect its dwindling water storages from
evaporation by covering them with millions of plastic balls (Howard
2015)---thus using petrochemicals in an attempt to solve a problem
originally caused by ... petrochemicals.

These **[examples illustrate the]{.underline}** 'wicked' character of
the **[complex challenges now facing the world's
cities]{.underline}**---**[where [poor]{.mark}ly-conceived
'[solutions']{.mark}]{.underline}** may only **[[land
the]{.underline}]{.mark}** metropolis, and the
[**[planet,]{.underline}** **[in]{.underline}**]{.mark} **[deeper
[trouble]{.mark} that it was before]{.underline}**. **[[This is]{.mark}
a direct [consequence of]{.mark} the pressure of demands from our
swollen [population outrunning]{.mark} the [natural
capacity]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[of the Earth]{.underline}** to supply
them, **[and short-sighted]{.underline}** or corrupt local **[politics
leading to 'bandaid' solutions that don't work or cause more trouble in
the long run.]{.underline}**

**[Other forms of increasing [urban vulnerability]{.mark}]{.underline}**
**[[include: storm]{.mark} damage, [sea level rise, flooding and
fire]{.mark} resulting from climate change]{.underline}** or geotectonic
forces; governance failure, **[[civic unrest]{.mark} and civil
war]{.underline}** exemplified in Lebanon, Iraq and Syria over the
2010s; disruption of oil supplies and consequent **[failure of food
supplies]{.underline}**; **[[worsening urban health problems]{.mark} due
to the rapid spread of pandemic diseases]{.underline}** and industrial
pollution and still ill-defined but real threats posed by the rise of
machine intelligence and nanoscience (Gencer 2013). The issue was
highlighted early in the present millennium by UN Secretary General Kofi
Annan, who wrote:

Communities will always face natural hazards, but **[today's disasters
are]{.underline}** often **[generated]{.underline}** by,
**[or]{.underline}** at least **[exacerbated by, human
activities]{.underline}**... **[At no time in]{.underline}** human
**[history have so many people lived in cities]{.underline}** clustered
around seismically active areas. Destitution and demographic pressure
have led more people than ever before to live in flood plains or in
areas prone to landslides. **[[Poor]{.mark} land-use planning;
[environmental management]{.mark}]{.underline}**; and a lack of
regulatory mechanisms both **[increase the risk and [exacerbate]{.mark}
the [effects of disasters]{.mark}]{.underline}** (Annan 2003).

**[[These]{.underline}]{.mark}** factors **[[are a warning sign
for]{.mark}]{.underline}** the real possibility of megacity
**[[collapses]{.underline}]{.mark}** within **[coming]{.underline}**
decades. With the universal spread of smart phones, the consequences
will be vividly displayed in real time on news bulletins and social
media. Unlike historic calamities, the whole world will have a virtual
ringside seat as **[future urban nightmares]{.underline}** unfold.

### Adv 2 -- AI Innovation

**Advantage 2 is AI Innovation**

**AI innovation is driving [unprecedented]{.underline} demand for
electricity.**

**Rus and Enriquez 9/24** -- Director, MIT Computer Science and
Artificial Intelligence Laboratory; Technology-to-Market Scholar at the
Energy Department's Advanced Research Projects Agency and student at the
Stanford Graduate School of Business. Daniela Rus and Nico Enriquez, "To
compete with China on AI, we need a lot more power," The Washington
Post, 09-24-2024,
https://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/2024/09/24/ai-power-grid-china-competition/

[Sophisticated]{.underline}, [impactful use of artificial intelligence
is vital for **holding our own against China**]{.underline} and other
rivals in an increasingly competitive world.

[But AI uses **a lot of electricity**]{.underline}. [A simple ChatGPT-4
query uses **more than 10 times the wattage** of a Google
search]{.underline}. The difference arises from complexity: The GPT
model utilizes 1.76 trillion parameters to predict each word in a
sentence, while Google uses just 4 to 10 million to rank screenfuls of
webpages. **[[America's aging]{.mark} power [grid isn't]{.mark} going to
be [able to handle AI's]{.mark} increasing [load]{.mark}]{.underline}**.

[[Imagine]{.mark} each electron used to power a single
computation]{.underline} --- [for a GPS route]{.underline}, say, [or a
medical test]{.underline}, [or a cybersecurity system]{.underline} ---
[is [a car]{.mark} trying to get home]{.underline}. [Today]{.underline},
[that car must make its way [on]{.mark}]{.underline} a complicated
network of aluminum power lines that are the equivalent of [[back
roads]{.underline}]{.mark}, [many]{.underline} of them
**[crowded]{.underline}**, [[with **no interstate**
highways]{.underline}]{.mark}. **[[That's]{.mark} the [U.S.]{.mark}
electricity [transmission]{.mark} system]{.underline}**: [more than a
**century old**]{.underline}, [patching together **3,200 local and
regional utilities**]{.underline}, [struggling to carry **even the
existing load**]{.underline}.

[Before we can use the **most advanced microchips** and run our
**state-of-the-art AI models**]{.underline}, [[we need
**interstate**]{.mark}**-type [corridors]{.mark}** [to transmit]{.mark}
sufficient]{.underline}, [reliable [power to]{.mark} our data
centers]{.underline}. [More than 150 [AI]{.mark} experts]{.underline}
that we have interviewed [in the last year affirmed]{.underline} that
[[our]{.mark} outdated electric [grid]{.mark} capacity [is **holding us
back**]{.mark}]{.underline}. [[If]{.mark} America's computer [servers
**hit their limits**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [suddenly the GPS in your car
will take a while to load]{.underline}. [The robotics assisting your
gall bladder surgery will pause to compute]{.underline}. [Even
**[critical]{.mark} cybersecurity and [infra]{.mark}structure** systems
[could be affected]{.mark}]{.underline}.

[It takes]{.underline} the United States 10 to 20 [years to get approval
for]{.underline} and build [new transmission]{.underline} lines.
[Compare that to China]{.underline}'s autocratic centralized efficiency:
Beijing has largely consolidated its regional utilities into one
state-run organization, and it can build new power lines in under five
years. [China]{.underline} now has a power system with a speed and scale
that may be challenging for the United States to match; [from 2014 to
2021]{.underline} China [built 80 times the interregional grid capacity
that we did]{.underline}.

China is also the fastest builder of energy generation in the world. In
the 11 years it took us to add two reactors to the Vogtle nuclear plant
near Augusta, Ga., our only new reactors in three decades, China built
almost 40. Meanwhile, between 2022 and 2023 China added more solar
capacity than the rest of the world combined.

[Our new "hyperscale" [data]{.mark} [center]{.mark} campuse[s]{.mark}
consume massive amounts of electricity]{.underline}. [Amazon's
development outside Pittsburgh uses more electricity than **750,000
homes**]{.underline}. [Microsoft and OpenAI are planning a "Stargate"
campus that would use **more power than 3 million homes**]{.underline}.
[These sites]{.underline} often [[**need** to be]{.mark} in or [near
**population centers**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[where]{.mark} our [grid
is **already stretched to its limit**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

We must innovate to assure we can power such concentrated computation.
First, the fastest and greenest way to address the problem is to use
less power. So we must build more efficient AI software and better
computer chips.

This goal is within our reach: Our research at MIT has resulted in
smaller, physics-based AI models that use 1/1000th the energy of ChatGPT
--- yet yield more accurate results. This exponential improvement would
mean that calculations that now require supercomputers could run on our
laptops and phones. And our colleagues have built computer chips that
use a tenth the energy of the world's best AI chips while yielding the
same results. As such innovations are put into use, an AI calculation
could become no more energy-intensive than a Google query.

Second, [we must]{.underline} more quickly [upgrade our power
transmission system]{.underline}. This means encouraging utilities to be
more efficient. Several safe, proven and low-cost "grid enhancing
technologies" could save taxpayers billions if widely deployed. One
example is upgrading existing metal power conductors by using new
composite materials that can carry two or three times more electricity;
a recent study estimated they could triple grid capacity at 25 percent
of the cost. Furthermore, because they would be using existing
transmission towers, they could be deployed in one to five years. Many
countries in Europe and Asia already use these systems.

Regulators should encourage these more efficient investments by changing
laws so utilities can profit from them --- for example, by letting them
retain 30 cents of every dollar they save for their customers. Britain
has instituted this incentive system to much success.

Last, [[we must]{.mark} learn from China and **[create national
high-voltage transmission]{.mark}** corridors]{.underline}. To support
this, we should take some of the \$150 billion our tech companies devote
annually to U.S. data center construction and pool it with federal and
utility funding. This year, the Energy Department designated 10 National
Interest Electric Transmission Corridors with access to inexpensive
federal loans and faster permit processes because they would
significantly improve our grid's reliability and reduce consumer energy
costs. We should expand these corridors to cover our nation.

To accelerate state and local approvals for transmission lines, Congress
should pass legislation proposed this summer by Sens. Joe Manchin III
(I-W.Va.) and John Barrasso (R-Wyo.) to speed environmental reviews,
limit local comment periods and properly compensate people who have
lines built in their backyards.

Much of the United States' economic growth during the 2000s has come
from our global leadership in tech. [AI is the next wave]{.underline}.
[The United States has the talent]{.underline}, [investor
base]{.underline}, [corporations and research institutions to write
**the most advanced AI** models]{.underline}. [But **without a powerful
data highway** system]{.underline}, [our great technology advances will
be **confined to back roads**]{.underline}.

**Electricity supply is the [crucial linchpin]{.underline} to US AI
dominance.**

Initiatives to Accelerate the Development of Geothermal Energy
