#### **Status quo climate change initiatives are insufficient to combat Arctic warming -- An Arctic geothermal policy is key**

**INN '24** (Innovation News Network is run by a qualified team of
editors dedicated to journalism regarding emerging technology,
innovation, policy, and more; "Are global policies doing enough to
combat Arctic climate change?"; 12/23/2024;
https://www.innovationnewsnetwork.com/are-global-policies-doing-enough-to-combat-arctic-climate-change/54086/)
RCV

[[The Arctic]{.mark} region [is]{.mark} [experiencing]{.mark} some of
the most dramatic and [rapid changes in]{.mark} the global
[climate]{.mark}. As the Earth's northernmost region, it is particularly
vulnerable to the impacts of climate change, with [temp]{.mark}erature[s
rising at]{.mark} more than [twice the global
average]{.mark}.]{.underline}

This phenomenon, known as Arctic amplification1, has profound
consequences not only for local ecosystems and Indigenous communities
but also for global weather patterns, sea levels, and biodiversity.

[[Combating]{.mark} Arctic [c]{.mark}limate [c]{.mark}hange
[requires]{.mark} [urgent]{.mark} and comprehensive [policy
response]{.mark}s [at]{.mark} local, [national]{.mark}, and
international [levels]{.mark}.]{.underline}

[This article discusses several key [policies]{.mark} and strategies
[that have been implemented]{.mark} and improvements to help further
address this pressing issue.]{.underline}

International policy to combat Arctic climate change

Worldwide policies and initiatives to combat Arctic climate change
include:

EU Arctic Policy

The EU aims to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and promote renewable
energy sources in the Arctic. It also supports the Arctic Council's goal
of reducing black carbon emissions by 33% by 2025.

UK Glasgow Climate Pact

The UK's policy framework aims to reduce emissions and achieve a net
zero transition. The UK also recognises the need to protect the Arctic
environment from increased activity.

Arctic Council Strategic Plan

The Arctic Council's plan promotes conservation and sustainable use of
the Arctic marine environment. The plan also encourages safety at sea,
prevention of marine pollution, and cooperation to improve knowledge of
the Arctic marine environment.

WWF Arctic

The WWF's Arctic Plan advocates for Arctic states to establish Arctic
Marine Protected Areas (MPAs) and Other Effective Area-Based
Conservation Measures (OECMs).

We need change, now

[[Despite these measures, there is still a long way to go]{.mark} in
combating Arctic climate change.]{.underline}

[What happens in the Arctic will influence the rest of our planet.
Without urgent action to slash greenhouse gas emissions, the world will
continue to feel the effects of a warming Arctic.]{.underline}

**\[IMAGE OMITTED\]**

For areas around the world---even thousands of kilometres south of the
Arctic---this will mean rising sea levels, changing temperature and
precipitation patterns, and more severe weather events.

Let's look at some policy improvements which could help reduce Arctic
climate change.

Strengthening global climate agreements

The Paris Agreement, which aims to limit global warming to below 2°C
(preferably to 1.5°C) above pre-industrial levels, is one of the primary
international mechanisms to combat climate change.

However, while the Agreement provides a framework for global
cooperation, it needs to be more robustly enforced, particularly in the
Arctic. Policies to strengthen commitments to climate mitigation, with
specific attention to emissions reduction in high-impact regions like
the Arctic, are essential.

Governments can push for stronger targets and timetables for carbon
emissions reductions, ensuring that the Arctic's vulnerability is fully
considered in future climate talks.

In particular, the international community needs to address the need for
action on short-lived climate pollutants (SLCPs) like black carbon and
methane, which are disproportionately affecting the Arctic.

By prioritising reductions in these pollutants, we can significantly
reduce regional warming.

Investing in renewable energy and green technologies

One of the most effective ways to reduce global warming is by
transitioning from fossil fuels to renewable energy sources.

[[The Arctic]{.mark} region, often seen as rich in oil and gas reserves,
[has been a focus of]{.mark} resource [extraction]{.mark}. However,
continued fossil fuel exploration and exploitation are accelerating
climate change in the region.]{.underline}

[[A shift in policy is needed to incentivise the development]{.mark} and
deployment [of renewable energy tech]{.mark}nologies, [such as]{.mark}
wind, solar, and [geothermal energy]{.mark}. Governments can introduce
tax credits, subsidies, and grants [to promote green]{.mark} energy
[solutions]{.mark}, not only in the Arctic but also in countries with
Arctic territories, like Canada, the United States, Russia, and
Norway.]{.underline}

[Moreover, promoting innovation in energy storage, grid integration,
[and]{.mark} low-carbon technologies can help these regions [transition
to a low-carbon economy]{.mark}]{.underline}.

#### U.S. geothermal efforts are lagging behind, despite progress in the energy sector

**Sweeney and Gordon '25** (Robert W. Sweeney is the managing partner of
Accelex Resources, an energy and minerals venture investor, and former
bank CEO with a thirty-five-year career in international finance and
Noah Gordon is the fellow in the Sustainability, Climate, and
Geopolitics Program at the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace in
Washington, DC; "Geothermal Energy and U.S. Competitive Advantage:
Drill, Baby, Drill"; 3/13/2025;
https://carnegieendowment.org/research/2025/03/geothermal-energy-and-us-competitive-advantage-drill-baby-drill?lang=en)
RCV

In this dynamic context, [[U.S. energy policy has evolved]{.mark}
significantly in terms of markets, supplies, regulation, and
legislation.]{.underline} The country's success in using fracking
technology and exploiting abundant shale reserves have made it the
world's largest producer of hydrocarbons and a major exporter,
especially of natural gas.1 [Further, [as]{.mark} national and
international [concerns about climate change have grown,]{.mark} U.S.
energy [policy]{.mark} [has become more aligned with
transitioning]{.mark} [to]{.mark} renewable sources. So far, the focus
of clean energy additions has been on [wind, solar, and nuclear
power.]{.mark}]{.underline} Beginning in the mid-2000s, the United
States issued legislation and policies raising renewable energy to the
level of industrial policy. Among those were the Energy Policy Act
(2005), the American Recovery and Reinvestment Act (2009), the
Infrastructure Investment and Jobs Act (IIJA) (2021), the CHIPS and
Science Act (2022), and the Inflation Reduction Act (IRA) (2022).

[[However, geothermal energy]{.mark}---which uses the heat of the
earth's crust for power---[has been]{.mark} largely [left out of
U.S.]{.mark} industrial [policy]{.mark}. This is [despite the facts that
utilizing this source]{.mark} of renewable energy [requires]{.mark} some
of [the same technologies that have made the U]{.mark}nited
[S]{.mark}tates [the world's top oil and gas producer]{.mark}
[and]{.mark} [that geothermal has the potential to provide
clean]{.mark}, dispatchable [power]{.mark} [that does not rely on
weather]{.mark} conditions.]{.underline}

[With U.S. clean firm power demand expected to increase by approximately
700--900 gigawatts by 2050,2 [the]{.mark} [U]{.mark}nited
[S]{.mark}tates [needs to]{.mark} dramatically [increase]{.mark}
[capacity]{.mark} [while reducing]{.mark} or eliminating net [carbon
output]{.mark} and insulating its energy supply from dependence on
international supply chains.]{.underline} The question for the country
now is: how should public and private resources be directed to provide
the United States with an energy system optimized for the current
national and international environment?

### Arctic Key

#### Arctic region key leader in [renewable modelling]{.underline} -- Alaska [proves]{.underline}. 

**DoE '22** \[U.S. Department of Energy. "Arctic Strategy," U.S.
Department of Energy Strategy to Support Arctic Activities and
Priorities, October 2022.
https://www.energy.gov/sites/default/files/2022-11/Arctic%20Strategy_1.pdf\]

[As a matter of necessity]{.underline}, [the **Arctic region** has
become a **leader** in **renewable** energy **development** and
**integration**]{.underline}, [with more than **double** the global
average in the percentage of power generated from renewable
resources]{.underline}. Countries like Iceland and Norway source
virtually 100% of their energy for heat and power from renewable
resources. [The **U**nited **S**tates is actively working with partners
across the region to **share** best **practices** and **enhance** the
region's overall **energy resilience**]{.underline}. In an estimated 250
locations, [diesel fuel is augmented with local]{.underline} sources of
renewable energy such as hydropower, wind, solar, biomass, marine
hydrokinetic or **[geothermal energy]{.underline}**. [Alaska has played
a **leadership role** in **incorporating** renewable
resources]{.underline} into community-scale microgrids.

#### **Geothermal energy systems are key to resilience for Arctic communities**

**Kolker et al '22** (Amanda Kolker is the laboratory program manager
for geothermal energy at the National Renewable Energy Laboratory
(NREL), Benjamin Anderson is a researcher at NREL, Karina Zyatitsky was
a researcher at NREL who now is attending graduate school at the
University of Utah, Timothy Reber is the Project Lead for International
Programs at NREL, Robbin Garber-Slaght is a researcher at NREL with a MS
in Cold Climate Engineering from the University of Alaska Fairbanks and
a BS in Mechanical Engineering also from the University of Alaska
Fairbanks, and Hannah Pauling is a member of the Thermal Energy Science
and Technologies group in the Energy Conversion and Storage Systems
Center at NREL; "Geothermal Energy and Resilience in Arctic Countries";
April 2022; https://docs.nrel.gov/docs/fy22osti/80928.pdf) RCV

[[Energy systems in remote communities in Arctic countries]{.mark} [can
be viewed as]{.mark} integrated heatpower-food systems
[essential]{.mark} [for]{.mark} community [survival]{.mark} and
resilience. [Conventional]{.mark} energy [systems]{.mark} in Arctic
countries [are susceptible to disruptions]{.mark}---both [grid-connected
and remote communitie]{.mark}s are likely to [face]{.mark} continuing
[threats from]{.mark} severe [weather]{.mark} events, [supply chain
disruptions]{.mark}, unstable [and]{.mark} [increasing diesel
cost]{.mark}, etc]{.underline}. The question this paper seeks to answer
is: are integrated geothermal energy systems resilient solutions for
communities in Arctic countries? The answer appears to be yes.
[[Geothermal is]{.mark} a [resilient]{.mark} energy source for power
[and]{.mark} heat, and in turn, its use [can]{.mark} [enhance]{.mark}
[the resilience of communities in Arctic countries]{.mark}. This is true
in smaller, isolated microgrid contexts as well. Although the economics
of small-scale geothermal applications may remain a barrier today
(though one that is getting smaller with each new technological
advancement), from the perspective of technical viability, reliability,
and sustainability, geothermal can play a significant role in providing
resilient energy solutions to remote communities in areas where
resources are present (see Figure 5). Notably[, geothermal offers a
known technology that is reliant only on locally occurring
resources]{.mark} (and thus independent of outside fuel supplies) [and
can supply]{.mark} dispatchable and flexible baseload heat and
[power]{.mark}---[a unique mix of characteristics that no other energy
source can yet lay claim to]{.mark}.]{.underline} Limited deployment of
geothermal microgrids in off-grid settings means that many unknowns
remain about their performance, but the success of large grid-connected
geothermal in providing ancillary services---particularly critical to
microgrid operation---and the success of small geothermal in providing
inexpensive power to remote communities are promising steps.

Nairobi has already become a boomtown for such development

[[Penetration of geothermal has been limited due to the low-cost of
fossil fuels]{.mark}. However, with the right incentives and forthcoming
carbon tax schemes geothermal could appear increasingly
attractive.]{.underline} [[Geothermal]{.underline} [provides a
low-carbon baseload power, which importantly offers grid stability,
meaning it could provide a realistic pathway to the closure of coal and
gas power plants10]{.underline}]{.mark},11. To date, the [preference of
the, apparently, cheaper wind and solar-PV technologies, unfortunately,
lock economies into a carbon-based gas or coal infrastructure38,
precisely because wind and Solar-PV lack the baseload component.
[Geothermal power is also flexible, allowing power plant operators to
ramp up and down the resource, therefore making it the perfect
low-carbon partner]{.mark}]{.underline} with wind and solar-PV and
hydroelectric resources. [Given the geotectonic position of several
world's megacities, we see a huge potential for the use of geothermal
power which would also reduce the environmental impact and help
contrasting the energy challenge in overpopulated areas]{.underline}.
Regarding regulation, there are a series of legal issues which are a
good guide for the proper regulation of this resource (e.g., royalty
payments, use fees, environmental protection, freedom of Information,
rights-of-way under the land policy and management, minerals management,
leasing, operations, rights of local communities, etc.) that are
permanently updated by countries and states that have a developed
geothermal industry (see, for example, the USA code of the state and

or that foresee the potential use of this resource in the medium or long

#### We have the capability to drill to access Geothermal energy needed to meet demand

**Winn 2022** \[Zach \| MIT News Office Tapping into the million-year
energy source below our feet
<https://news.mit.edu/2022/quaise-energy-geothermal-0628> Accessed
2/8/2025\]

[There's an abandoned coal power plant in upstate New York that most
people regard as a useless relic]{.underline}. But MIT's Paul Woskov
sees things differently. [Woskov, a research engineer in MIT's Plasma
Science and Fusion Center, notes [the plant's power turbine is still
intact and the transmission lines still run to the
grid.]{.mark}]{.underline} Using an approach he's been working on for
the last 14 years, he's hoping it [will be back online, completely
carbon-free, within the decade.]{.underline} In fact, Quaise Energy, the
company commercializing Woskov's work, believes [[if it can retrofit one
power plant, the same process will work on virtually every coal and gas
power plant]{.underline}]{.mark} in the world. [Quaise is hoping to
accomplish those lofty goals by tapping into the energy source below our
feet.]{.underline} [[The company plans to vaporize enough rock to create
the world's deepest holes and harvest **geothermal** energy at a scale
that could satisfy human energy consumption for millions of
years]{.mark}. T]{.underline}hey haven't yet solved all the related
engineering challenges, but Quaise's founders have set an ambitious
timeline to begin harvesting energy from a pilot well by 2026. The plan
would be easier to dismiss as unrealistic if it were based on a new and
unproven technology. [But Quaise's drilling systems center around a
microwave-emitting device called a gyrotron that has been used in
research and manufacturing for decades.]{.underline} "This will happen
quickly once we solve the immediate engineering problems of transmitting
a clean beam and having it operate at a high energy density without
breakdown," explains Woskov, who is not formally affiliated with Quaise
but serves as an advisor. "[[It'll go fast because the underlying
technology, gyrotrons, are commercially available]{.mark}. You could
place an order with a company and have a system delivered right now ---
granted, these beam sources have never been used 24/7, but [they are
engineered to be operational for long time periods]{.mark}. In five or
six years, I think we'll have a plant running]{.underline} if we solve
these engineering problems. I'm very optimistic." [Woskov and many other
researchers have been using gyrotrons to heat material in nuclear fusion
experiments for decades. It wasn't until 2008, however, after the MIT
Energy Initiative (MITEI) published a request for proposals on new
geothermal drilling technologies]{.underline}, that Woskov thought of
using gyrotrons for a new application. "\[Gyrotrons\] haven't been
well-publicized in the general science community, but [[those of us in
fusion research understood they were very powerful beam sources --- like
lasers, but in a different frequency range,"]{.mark} Woskov says. "I
thought, [why not direct these high-power beams, instead of into fusion
plasma, down into rock and vaporize]{.mark} the hole]{.underline}?" As
power from other renewable energy sources has exploded in recent
decades, [geothermal energy has plateaued, mainly because geothermal
plants only exist in places where natural conditions allow for energy
extraction at relatively shallow depths of up to 400 feet beneath the
Earth's surface. At a certain point, conventional drilling becomes
impractical because deeper crust is both hotter and harder, which wears
down mechanical drill bits]{.underline}. Woskov's idea to use gyrotron
beams to vaporize rock sent him on a research journey that has never
really stopped. With some funding from MITEI, he began running tests,
quickly filling his office with small rock formations he'd blasted with
millimeter waves from a small gyrotron in MIT's Plasma Science and
Fusion Center.

[Geothermal projects already carry relatively higher fixed capital

are disincentivized to support new projects. However, [[when that risk
is minimized and projects become more profitable, deployment
accelerates]{.mark}, giving domestic developers more opportunities to

scale.]{.underline}

[This could be accomplished by DOE reimplementing and Congress

GTO like those phased out in the 1980s, such as the Program Research
Development Announcement (PRDA, 1976), Program Opportunity Notice (PON,
1979), and User-Coupled Confirmation Drilling Program (UCDP, 1980).8
Additionally, access to DOE financing programs specifically for
geothermal energy projects like the 1974 Geothermal Loan Guarantee
Program and 1980 Loans for Geothermal Reservoir Confirmation Program may
also be helpful.9 [Improved versions of these programs could ensure
percentages of available loan authorities are applied to independent
stages of EGS developmen]{.underline}t like lease acquisition,
exploratory drilling, development drilling, or power plant
construction.10 [While DOE's Loan Programs Office currently provides
loan guarantees for clean energy projects, including geothermal
technologies, available funds are not specifically parsed out for
different technologies or stages of project development]{.underline},
and for the most part come with additional statutory requirements that
are absent from these prior programs, such as innovation or
site-specific stipulations.11 These loans would have an even greater
impact if they could be paired with or converted into grants supported
by a Congressionally appropriated geothermal risk reduction fund, to
offset exploratory drilling risks, making public and private debt more
feasible for the industry.

[Streamline barriers to domestic deployment. A favorable permitting and
regulatory environment can unlock geothermal deployment, facilitate
learning, and help US companies achieve economies of scale]{.underline}.
According to BCG, [it takes an average of 7-10 years to develop
geothermal plants, and 4-6 of those years are spent mainly on well
permitting]{.underline}.12 In contrast, the average deployment timelines
for both O&G and utility-scale wind and solar projects are 2-6 years.13
[Longer timelines compound the cost of capital, with financing making up
30% of geothermal's already high capital costs]{.underline}.14 When
compared to 2-7% for wind and solar, the [appetite for private

**Conathan '22** \[[Michael Conathan]{.underline}, Senior Policy Fellow,

[As the **U.S.** considers expanding its **military** **op**eration**s**

one presenter described as "chronically underfunded"---[it should do so
in a way that does not **exacerbate** the effects of **climate
change**]{.underline}. **[Energy]{.underline}**, of course, [is
**essential** to **military** **op**eration**s**]{.underline}, and one
presenter stipulated that [fuel consumption by the **U.S.** armed forces
represents **2 percent** of **all** global fuel use]{.underline}.
[Presenters agreed that opportunities for **military decarbonization**
could drive change in **key areas**]{.underline} [including]{.underline}
microgrids and distribution of decarbonization infrastructure, electric
vehicle and charging station infrastructure, [**expansion** of
**geothermal** energy supply]{.underline}, safe deployment of nuclear
power including micro-reactors, and acceleration of demand for
sustainable shipping and aviation fuel.

Conversely, [continued **reliance** on traditional **fossil energy**
sources has negatively affected force **readiness** as the **fuel price
spikes** can literally cost the Department of Defense **billions** of
dollars in unforeseen and unbudgeted expenses]{.underline}. One
participant acknowledged "that's a big number, even for D.o.D."

As one presenter put it, "climate change is not a new issue for the
military." The Department of Defense has seen and felt its implications
from sea-level rise threatening infrastructure like the world's largest
naval base in Norfolk, Virginia to the spike in humanitarian assistance
and disaster relief missions related to extreme weather events. Today,
military operations are already at the cutting edge of U.S. renewable
energy implementation. Two-thirds of energy consumed on bases, 1.2
gigawatts annually, comes from renewable sources and saves \$400 million
per year in energy costs. And [leadership believes that accelerating the
transition to renewables in the long-term will]{.underline} "[give us a
**war-fighting advantage** and protect **national
security**]{.underline} [from]{.underline} the biggest threat we face:
**[climate change]{.underline}**."

U.S. Energy Choices

Of course, climate change is not just a challenge for military
operations; it also threatens global society as a whole. So, what energy
choices should the United States make now to address the threat and
pivot to a clean energy future? On these points, experts presented on
technologies that can accelerate America's transition and ways to
optimize our energy grid to handle new sources of electricity and avoid
the extreme weather-fueled breakdowns we have witnessed in recent years,
most notably in Texas and California. On the former point, one expert
broke the challenges down to six factors: scalability, affordability,
reliability, carbon and "traditional" pollution reduction, position on
the learning curve, and position on the price curve. Of the options
currently under consideration, this presenter ranked solar, wind,
offshore wind, EVs, batteries, and heat pumps as technologies that are
ready today for large scale deployment. However, there are key
[constraining factors]{.underline}, such as siting and deployment
challenges for solar and wind, and minerals and installer/labor
availability [for]{.underline} the others. Solar thermal energy,
**[geothermal energy]{.underline}**, hydrogen, and storage tech were in
the next tier, with the main challenges to deployment being research and
development investment. The presenter ranked other options in the
"future" (4th generation nuclear and carbon capture and storage) and
"pipe dream" (nuclear fusion and direct air capture) categories.

The Inflation Reduction Act took significant steps to begin addressing
many of these challenges, yet the major blockade to rapid deployment,
particular for the "ready" suite of technologies, [comes down to issues
of **siting** and **permitting**]{.underline}. [Across the spectrum of
members of **Congress**]{.underline} and presenters alike, there [was
clear agreement that the pace of **permitting** is a major
**hurdle**]{.underline}, [and it should be addressed if the **U.S.** is
to **keep up** with other developed countries]{.underline} in renewable
energy deployment.

### AT: CCS CP

#### CCS fails -- too small, expensive and uses too much energy

Jonathan **Foley 24** Is the Executive Director Of Project Drawdown, A
Nonprofit Organization Focused On Climate Solutions. His Writing Has
Been Featured On The Ted Stage and In National Geographic, Science,
Nature And Numerous Other Publications., 8-27-2024, \"Don\'t Fall for
Big Oil\'s Carbon Capture Deceptions\",
<https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/dont-fall-for-big-oils-carbon-capture-deceptions/>,
snapshot of page from 8/27/24, +=

In the inevitable crescendo of hype and [[greenwashing
that's]{.mark}]{.underline} coming our way, we'll doubtless hear a lot
about [[industrial carbon capture]{.underline}]{.mark} technologies that
attempt to remove carbon dioxide from the atmosphere. The COP 28 host
country, the United Arab Emirates, the world's largest oil companies and
even programs in the U.S. Department of Energy are working hard to
pushthis stuff.

Don't be fooled. It's mostly a distraction from what we really need to
do right now: phase out fossil fuels and deploy more effective climate
solutions.

Industrial carbon capture technologies come in many flavors, but the
most prominent are carbon capture and storage (CCS), which removes
carbon dioxide from highly concentrated point sources like power plants,
and direct air capture (DAC), which attempts to remove CO2 from open
air, where concentrations are much lower.

At first blush, this sounds great. But, as I've written previously,
counting on these technologies today is a bad idea. First, [[industrial
c]{.underline}]{.mark}arbon [[c]{.underline}]{.mark}apture projects
[[are]{.underline}]{.mark} far [[too small to
matter]{.underline}]{.mark}. [[Even after decades of
investment]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[r]{.underline}]{.mark}esearch
[[and]{.underline}]{.mark} [[d]{.underline}]{.mark}evelopment, [[today's
largest carbon capture projects **only remove a few seconds' worth of
our yearly** g]{.underline}]{.mark}reen[[h]{.underline}]{.mark}ouse
[[g]{.underline}]{.mark}a[[s]{.mark} emissions]{.underline}. And even
the planned Regional Direct Air Capture Hubs the Department of Energy is
supportingwill only be able to capture one million metric tonnes of CO2
every year; last year, the world emitted 40.5 billion.

Second, they are [[far too expensive]{.underline}]{.mark}, costing
thousands of dollars for every ton of CO2 removed.
[[Other]{.underline}]{.mark} climate [[solutions]{.underline}]{.mark},
including improving [[energy efficiency]{.underline}]{.mark}, deploying
[[renewable]{.underline}]{.mark} energy source[[s]{.underline}]{.mark}
and addressing emissions in agriculture and industrial sectors, [[are
far more cost-effective]{.underline}]{.mark}. Industrial carbon removal
costs at least \$1,000 per tonne removed; many other climate solutions
either have costs lower than \$10 per ton, and some have negative costs,
saving money immediately.

Third, these industrial carbon removal techniques also [[consume
excessive amounts of energy]{.underline}]{.mark}, which [[present
enormous challenges to scalability]{.underline}]{.mark}. If we power
carbon capture projects with CO2-spewing fossil fuels, the projects lose
much of their proposed climate benefit. Moreover, powering them with
renewable or nuclear energy sources would provide far less climate
benefit than using that energy to directly displace fossil fuels.

In addition, CO2 captured by industrial carbon capture projects is
[[often used to drive more oil and gas back
out]{.underline}]{.mark}---for something known as enhanced oil recovery,
which uses fluids like carbon dioxide to push oil and gas out of rock
formations---[[helping fossil fuel companies continue
working.]{.underline}]{.mark}

The (Limited) Role of Carbon Capture

In September, the I.E.A. published a detailed road map for what it would
take to slash the world's energy-related emissions to nearly zero by
midcentury in order to lessen the risk of catastrophic climate
disruptions.

In that road map, carbon capture accounted for just 8 percent of the
world's total emissions cuts between today and 2050. By contrast, the
vast majority of reductions would come from countries shifting away from
fossil fuels entirely: relying more heavily on wind and solar power for
electricity and swapping out gasoline-powered cars for electric ones.

Cost is one reason. In theory, [[companies could attach a carbon capture
device]{.underline}]{.mark} onto almost any factory or power plant that
burns fossil fuels today. But in practice, [[it's]{.mark}]{.underline}
often [[cheaper to shut down a coal plant and replace it
with]{.underline}]{.mark} some combination of [[wind,
solar]{.underline}]{.mark} and batteries, or to swap out a gas boiler
for an electric heat pump.

capture [[would **require**]{.mark} nearly [the **entire** **electricity
output** of the **U**]{.mark}nited **[S]{.mark}**tates]{.underline}.

It's also important to consider the scale of what would be needed. The
Energy Department recently announced \$12 million to fund "direct air
capture" projects. It also touted the possible removal of 100,000 tons
of carbon dioxide from the atmosphere.

To put this in perspective, the largest corporate polluter in 2018 was
responsible for releasing 119 million tons of CO2 equivalent --- and
that's only one of them.

[3. Carbon Capture Actually Increases **Emissions**]{.underline}

[[Due to]{.mark} the **large amount** of [**energy**
required]{.mark}]{.underline} to power carbon capture, [plus the **life
cycle** of fossil fuels, **[carbon capture]{.mark}**]{.underline} in
this country [[has]{.mark} actually **[put more CO2 into the atmosphere
than it has removed.]{.mark}**]{.underline}

That's not an accident. "Succesful" capture projects only exist at
facilities where the carbon is injected into existing wells to extract
more oil. This practice is known as "enhanced oil recovery." While an
oil company CEO might argue that doubling down on fossil fuels is a
climate solution, the planet begs to differ.

[4. [Storage]{.mark} Presents Significant
**[Risk]{.mark}**]{.underline}s

**large amounts** of CO2]{.mark}. Storage locations can [leak]{.mark}
CO2]{.underline}, as they are often sited near fossil fuel reservoirs.
There, oil [and]{.underline} gas [**wellbores** provide a **pathway**
for CO2 to **escape** to the surface]{.underline}. Those [storage
leaks]{.underline} could [[contaminate]{.mark} ground[water and
soil.]{.mark}]{.underline} Moreover, CO2 [injections could
[cause]{.mark} **[earthquakes]{.mark}**]{.underline}, which have already
been measured at injection sites.

As Friends of the Earth noted recently, when a CO2 pipeline in a
majority Black community in Mississippi ruptured last year, residents
had to seek medical treatment. The incident also killed local plants and
wildlife.

[[Counting on **carbon capture**]{.mark}**'s effectiveness**
[squanders]{.mark} the [**opportunity** to enact]{.mark} actual

Nuclear warfare

The likelihood that nuclear war could break out is higher than it was a
decade ago, the experts warned. In the wake of the Hiroshima bombing
which killed up to 150,000 people in the immediate aftermath, \"the
world has lived in the shadow of a war unlike any other in history,\"
they said.

Weapons with the highest yield have the power to obliterate 80 to 90
percent of lifeforms, including humans, in a 1-4 kilometer radius. With
around 7,000 warheads each, the U.S and Russia have the biggest
arsenals, with the U.K., France, China, India, Pakistan, North Korea and
Israel confirmed or believed to possess some form of nuclear device.

A nuclear war could not only wipe out lives and cities, and leave behind
the threat of radioactive disease, but the resulting fallout could
trigger a mini ice-age.

#### "Arctic" includes Aleutians.

**Comay** et al**. '25** \[Laura, John Frittelli, Emma Kaboli, Caitlin
Keating-Bitonti, Anthony Marshak, Mariel Murray, Ronald O'Rourke,
Jonathan L. Ramseur, Lexie Ryan, and Pervaze A. Sheikh; June 18;

Arctic Circle Definition

[The **[most common]{.mark}** and basic **[definition]{.mark}** of the
[**Arctic** defines]{.mark} the region as the [land and sea area north
of the Arctic Circle]{.mark}]{.underline} (a circle of [latitude at
about 66° 34′ North).3 For surface locations [within this zone]{.mark},
[the sun is]{.mark} generally above]{.underline} the horizon [[for 24
continuous hours at least once per year]{.underline}]{.mark} (at the
summer solstice) and below the horizon for 24 continuous hours at least
once per year (at the winter solstice). The land and sea area within the
Arctic Circle is about 8.14 million square miles,4 which is about 4.1%
(or between 1/24th and 1/25th) of the Earth\'s surface, and more than
twice the land area of the United States, which is about 3.5 million
square miles.

The Arctic Circle definition of the Arctic includes the northernmost
part of Alaska---more than one-quarter of the state\'s land area5---as
well as the Chukchi Sea, which separates that part of Alaska from
Russia, and U.S. territorial and Exclusive Economic Zone (EEZ) waters
north of Alaska. It does not include the more southerly parts of Alaska
or the Bering Sea, which separates that lower part of the state from
Russia.

[[The **Arctic Ocean**]{.underline}]{.mark}, which is roughly at the
center of the Arctic region, [[accounts for much of the region\'s total
area]{.underline}]{.mark}. By one calculation, the Arctic Ocean has an
area of about 6.01 million square miles, which is about 4.3% of the
Earth\'s ocean area.6 This figure uses boundaries for the Arctic Ocean
that include some waters south of the Arctic Circle.7 Other sources,
using different boundaries for the Arctic Ocean, put the size of the
Arctic Ocean at about 5.4 million square miles.8

Definition in [Arctic Research and Policy Act (ARPA) of
1984]{.underline}

[Section 112 of the Arctic Research and Policy Act (ARPA) of
1984]{.underline} (Title I of P.L. 98-373 of July 31, 1984)9 defines the
Arctic as follows:

As used in this title, [[the term \"**Arctic**\" means all
U]{.underline}]{.mark}nited **[[S]{.underline}]{.mark}**tates [[and
foreign territory north of the **Arctic Circle**]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[and all United States territory north and west of the]{.mark} boundary
formed by the [Porcupine, Yukon, and Kuskokwim Rivers]{.mark} \[in
Alaska\];]{.underline} all contiguous seas, including [[the Arctic
Ocean]{.underline}]{.mark} and the [Beaufort, Bering, and Chukchi Seas;
[and]{.mark} the **[Aleutian chain]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

[This definition, which is [**codified** at]{.mark} **15 U.S.C.
4111,10** includes certain parts of Alaska below the Arctic
Circle]{.underline}, including the Aleutian Islands and portions of
central and western mainland Alaska, such as the Seward Peninsula and
the Yukon Delta.

The U.S. Coast Guard states that \"The U.S. Arctic encompasses some
2,521 miles of shoreline, an international strait adjacent to the
Russian Federation, and 647 miles of land border with Canada above the
Arctic Circle. The U.S. Exclusive Economic Zone (EEZ) in the Arctic
contains approximately 889,000 square miles of ocean.\"11 Figure 1 shows
the Arctic area of Alaska as defined by ARPA; Figure 2 shows the entire
Arctic area as defined by ARPA.

#### Arctic is territory north of the Arctic Circle as well as Alaska and the Aleutian Islands

**State Department, no date**. "Arctic Region"
https://2017-2021.state.gov/key-topics-office-of-ocean-and-polar-affairs/arctic/

Arctic Science Agreement (2017)

In May 2017, the eight Arctic States signed the Agreement on Enhancing
International Arctic Scientific Cooperation during the 10th Arctic
Council Ministerial in Fairbanks, Alaska. This is the third legally
binding agreement negotiated under the auspices of the Arctic Council.
The agreement facilitates access by scientists of the eight Arctic
States to Arctic areas that each State has identified, including entry
and exit of persons, equipment, and materials; access to research
infrastructure and facilities; and access to research areas. The
agreement also calls for the parties to promote education and training
of scientists working on Arctic matters.

[The]{.underline} geographic [area, as defined by the Arctic Research
and Policy Act]{.underline} of 1984 , covered by this agreement in the
United States [includes territory north of the Arctic Circle and north
and west of the boundary formed by the Porcupine, Yukon, and Kuskokwim
Rivers; the Aleutian chain; and adjacent marine areas in the Arctic
Ocean and the Beaufort, Bering, and Chukchi Seas.]{.underline}

#### Geothermal energy isn't extractive -- solving climate change turns the K. 

**Jones and Tait '23** (Bryant Jones is a science and technology studies
scholar at Boise State University. He researches energy transitions and
how energy advocacy coalitions frame narratives and establish boundaries
to get on policy agendas. He worked at both the White House and U.S.
Department of State and is a National Security Fellow with the Truman
National Security Project and Peter Tait is a California tech executive
and marketer turned renewable energy advocate. After a lifetime spent
next to the geothermal community, he has joined the movement to advocate
for geothermal energy as a long-term solution to the climate crisis;
"Empower the Geothermal Earthshot: Solve the Climate Crisis with Earth's
Energy"; January 9/2023;
https://fas.org/publication/solve-the-climate-crisis-with-earths-energy/)
RCV

[[Geothermal energy's]{.mark} primary [benefits make it]{.mark} an
[ideal]{.mark} energy candidate [in America's fight against]{.mark}
[c]{.mark}limate [c]{.mark}hange.]{.underline} First, geothermal
electricity offers [[clean]{.mark} firm, [reliable]{.mark}, [and
stable]{.mark} baseload [power]{.mark}]{.underline}. As such[, it easily
complements wind and solar energy]{.underline}, which can fluctuate and
produce only intermittent power. [Not only does geothermal energy offer
more resilient and renewable energy, but]{.underline}----unlike nuclear
and biomass energy and battery storage----[it does so [with no harmful
waste by-products]{.mark}. **[Geothermal energy does not depend on
extractive activities]{.mark}**]{.underline} (i.e., mining)
[[that]{.mark} have a history of [adversely]{.mark} [impact]{.mark}ing
[the environment and Indigenous communities]{.mark}.]{.underline} The
underlying energy source----the literal heat beneath our feet----is
local, is 100% American, and has demonstrated gigawatt-scale operation
since the 1980s, unlike every other prospective clean energy
technology[. [Geothermal energy offers]{.mark} a [tech]{.mark}nology
that we can export as a service provider and manufacturer to the rest of
the world [to reduce]{.mark} global [GHG emissions]{.mark}, [increase
U.S. energy independence]{.mark}, **[and improve the country's economy
and national defense]{.mark}**.]{.underline} Additionally, climate
change continues to change outside air temperatures and weather patterns
impacting building energy consumptions (e.g., heating and cooling),
which are expected to increase. Geothermal heating and cooling meets
these demands by providing reliable and distributed electricity
generation, winter heating, and summer cooling. Geothermal heating and
cooling offer solutions to other economic sectors that produce harmful
carbon and methane emissions. Getting to net-zero by 2050----and
eventually to 24/7 carbon-free energy----is a community problem, a
public sector problem that affects America's public health, economic
survival, and national security. We can get here if geothermal is
provided the same opportunities that the government has afforded all
other energy technologies. Geothermal Energy: The Forgotten Energy
Technology Today, geothermal power production is at the same
developmental stage that oil production was 100 years ago. Geothermal
power production has been proven at gigawatt scale, but in a limited
range of locations where conventional hydrothermal systems are easily
accessible. Petroleum drilling in the United States began in 1859 and
expanded first in places where oil was visible, easily identifiable, and
quickly accessible. In the 150 years since, continuous market support
from governments and societies has allowed the fossil fuel economy not
just to continue but to expand through technology innovation. Fossil
fuel technologies have matured to the point where engineers regularly
drill seven to eight miles underground, drill in deep ocean water, and
utilize efficient recovery technologies such as steam-assisted gravity
drainage. [[Geothermal]{.mark} carries the same potential to [drive new
technologies of energy production]{.mark} and enable huge increases in
energy recovery and output. However, unlike the petroleum industry,
[geothermal energy has never received]{.mark} comparable and effective
[policy support from the federal and state governments]{.mark} to drive
this needed technology development, innovation, and deployment. As a
result, the geothermal industry has been left behind in the United
States.]{.underline}

#### Understanding climate change as [existential]{.underline} does [not]{.underline} require understanding it as [binary]{.underline} or [linear]{.underline}. Instead, we should understand it as an [ongoing]{.underline}, [non-linear]{.underline} risk to the very [existence]{.underline} of humanity itself.

**Kemp & Beard '24** -- Lecturer in Environmental Policy, Australian
Nat'l U, and Ph.D. in Int'l Relations; Senior Research Associate &
Academic Programme Manager of the CSER, Cambridge, Luke Kemp, also
Faculty Fellow, Notre Dame Institute for Advanced Studies, and Research
Affiliate, Centre for the Study of Existential Risk (CSER), and SJ
Beard, also Associated Researcher, the Institute for Futures Studies,
"Chapter 14: Existential Change: Lesson from Climate Change for
Existential Risk," *An Anthology of Global Risk* (Cambridge, UK: Open
Book Publishers, 3 Sept. 2024), SJ Beard and Tom Hobson (eds.), pp.
403-404

Within Existential Risk Studies [it is **common** to hear people
ask]{.underline} the question ["**[is climate change]{.mark} an
[existential]{.mark} risk**?", and many who ask this question **answer
negatively**, **arguing** that as a]{.underline} [**result** climate
change is **not an important topic of research**]{.underline} within the
field. However, whether it is answered affirmatively or not, **[this
question is misguided]{.underline}**. There are three reasons for
thinking this. **[First]{.underline}**ly, [it makes **little sense** on
a **probabilistic level**; whether something will be a threat to our
collective existence [is **not**]{.mark} **a
[binary]{.mark}**]{.underline} matter, [[it is a question of
**likelihood**]{.underline}]{.mark}. [However, many researchers within
Existential Risk Studies **mistakenly conflict** existential risk with
events that could be existential catastrophes]{.underline}.
**[Second]{.underline}**ly, [[climate change is **not**]{.mark} **a
single [uniform]{.mark} process** that will affect **everyone** in the
**same way**; [it is]{.mark} a set of [**diffuse impacts** to
**different exposed** populations]{.mark}, **interacting** with
different **vulnerabilities**]{.underline} [and exposures, and
**[activating]{.mark}** different **[risk
cascades]{.mark}**]{.underline}. As Richards et al. show, [[it will
**inevitably** interact with]{.mark} a **host** of **other**
threats]{.underline} (not only [**[food security]{.mark}** and
**[societal collapse]{.mark}**, but even]{.underline} [factors such as
the explosivity of **[volcanic eruptions]{.mark}** or the emergence of
**[zoonotic pathogens]{.mark}**]{.underline}),1 [and **these** can
**interact with one another** [to create]{.mark} **reinforcing feedback
loops**]{.underline} [or "**[global systems death
spirals]{.mark}**]{.underline}".2 [**Finally**, " **existential risk**"
is **too vague** and **arbitrary** a concept for the question to ever be
answered. All the definitions of existential risk]{.underline} that have
received the greatest public attention thus far, [such as]{.underline}
Toby [**Ord's**, focused]{.underline} not in terms of an impact on
humanity at any point in time but rather [in terms of "the loss of
**long-term future value**]{.underline}";3 either [referring to the
author(s) particular vision of a high-tech intergalactic utopia, or a
fuzzy undefined idea of "our potential".]{.underline}4

Other authors have practised attribution substitution and sought to
answer an easier question such as "will the direct impacts of climate
change make the Earth uninhabitable?" as a proxy for existential risk,5
or suggested agricultural impossibility as a proxy for civilisational
collapse at a given level of temperature rise.6 These are certainly more
tractable questions, but they are also entirely different questions, and
there is a danger in thinking that answering them is sufficient to
assess the overall level of climate risk.

[[We are **better**]{.mark} **off** **[reverting]{.mark}** back [to
the]{.mark} **common-sense [definition]{.mark}** [of]{.mark}
[ex]{.mark}istential [risk]{.mark} [as]{.mark} the [**risk** to]{.mark}
the **[existence]{.mark}** of a given object]{.underline}, [[and
**specifying** whether the object]{.mark} under threat
[is]{.mark}]{.underline} [**[humanity]{.mark} as a whole** ( extinction
risk), **global [industrial society]{.mark}** (collapse risk), [or
**something else**]{.mark} entirely. We should be thinking of an
**overall level of risk** **emergent**]{.underline} [from a particular
socio-ecological system, and **how much** climate change **influences
this** level]{.underline}.7 And [[the **question** we should]{.mark} be
[ask]{.mark}ing about this risk [is **what contribution**]{.mark}**,
under certain scenarios**]{.underline}, **[[climate change will
make]{.underline}]{.mark}**, bearing in mind that it will almost
certainly be operating in tandem with many other drivers of risk.

[[Considering **this**]{.mark} **revised question**
[can]{.mark}]{.underline} also [help to [**rectify** a]{.mark}
**recurring [problem]{.mark}** in the climate risk literature:
**[using]{.mark} mean [global]{.mark} [temp]{.mark}erature** rise [as
the **sole**]{.mark} **threat [indicator]{.mark}**]{.underline}.
[Authors and activists alike have frequently made a direct link between
the level of warming and the likelihood of global catastrophe, with
**4--6 °C** being **most frequently used**]{.underline} [as this
terrible threshold.8]{.underline} [However, **global surface
[temp]{.mark}erature** [is **only one**]{.mark} of the climate change
induced [factor]{.mark}s we need to worry about.]{.underline} **[[3
°]{.mark}C]{.underline}** of warming above pre-industrial levels [[could
be]{.underline}]{.mark} [**entirely [manageable]{.mark}**
[**if**]{.mark} it occurs in a world of **[adaptive]{.mark}
[tech]{.mark}nologies**, high levels of multilateral cooperation, wealth
equality, trust in institutions, [and]{.mark} the **[safe
management]{.mark}** of other]{.underline} [planetary
boundaries]{.underline}. [[It could **also** be **catastrophic**]{.mark}
in a world [where other]{.mark}]{.underline} [**planetary
[boundaries]{.mark}** [are **transgressed**]{.mark}, [the]{.mark}
**international [order]{.mark}** [is **riven with
conflict**]{.mark}]{.underline}, lethal autonomous weapons are in mass
production, [and societies are scarred by inequality]{.underline}, low
trust, and polarization. [[Understanding]{.mark} the contribution of
climate change]{.underline} to Global Catastrophic Risk [[requires
a]{.underline}]{.mark} more [[**sophisticated** approach]{.mark} which
looks **beyond** the direct impacts of a given level of warming [to
**think through** **fully formed** **climate
scenarios**]{.mark}]{.underline}. We believe that, [when **conceived**
of [in **this** way]{.mark}, the **[risks]{.mark}** associated with
climate change [are **more appreciable** and it is]{.mark} **far [harder
to argue]{.mark}** that **[understanding]{.mark}** them [is
**unimportant**]{.mark}]{.underline}; however, even if others disagree
with this assessment, we still maintain that this is the right way to
think about the problem.

#### Climate change [outweighs]{.underline}. Our impacts guarantee elimination of the conditions of possibility for all politics and justice, which necessarily precedes individual vectors of injustice. The pluralist precautionary approach enables effective moral and political decision-making without eliding systemic harms nor being vulnerable to cooption.

**Mittiga 24** -- Researcher and Lecturer, Dept. of Philosophy,
University of Graz

Ross Mittiga, "Climate Change, Catastrophe, and the Circumstances of
Justice," Ch. 2 in *Climate Change as Political Catastrophe: Before
Collapse* *Climate Change as Political Catastrophe: Before Collapse*
(Oxford UPress, Feb. 2024), at pp. 18-46

[If we **continue** on [our **current path**]{.mark} we [will]{.mark}
face the **[collapse]{.mark}** of **[everything]{.mark}**]{.underline}
that gives us our security: [**[food]{.mark}** production, access to
fresh **[water]{.mark}**, [habitable]{.mark} ambient
**[temp]{.mark}erature**, and **[ocean]{.mark}** food chain[s]{.mark}.
And if the **natural world** can no longer support the **most basic** of
our needs then]{.underline} much of [the rest of **civilization** will
**quickly break down**]{.underline}. Please [make no mistake: [**climate
